Zusammenfassung 
iv 
Zusammenfassung 
Diese Masterarbeit, welche Teil meines Masterstudiums “Energie- und Verfahrenstechnik” an der 
Technischen Universität Berlin ist, wurde an der Technical University of Denmark, DTU, erstellt. Die 
Betreuung an der DTU erfolgte durch Lasse Røngaard Clausen und an der TU Berlin durch Prof. 
George Tsatsaronis. 
Die Arbeit wurde auf Englisch erstellt und in zwei getrennte Teile unterteilt. In Teil 1 wird ein hybrides 
Energiespeichersystem, basierend auf reversiblen Hochdruck-Festoxid-Brennstoffzellen (SOC) und 
Biomassevergasung untersucht. In Teil 2 wird ein, in einen Wärmetauscher integrierter SOC-Stack, 
welche für das in Teil 1 präsentierte System oder für andere Hochdruck-SOC-Systeme verwendet 
werden kann, entworfen und analysiert. 
Beide Teile sind identisch aufgebaut mit einem Abstract, einem Hauptteil, bestehend aus Einleitung, 
Methodik, Ergebnissen, Diskussion und Schluss, und einem Anhang. 
Das Ziel der Arbeit war es, ein zuvor vorgeschlagenes Energiespeichersystem, welches auf 
reversiblen SOCs basiert, durch einen Biomassevergaser zu erweitern. Dies ermöglicht eine 
flexiblere Betriebsweise des Systems und die Produktion von zusätzlichem synthetischen Erdgas, 
welches in das bestehende Erdgasnetz eingespeist werden kann. Zudem wird ein innovativer, 
Wärmetauscher für Hochdruckanwendungen entworfen, welcher den hohen Drücken und 
Temperaturen der SOC standhält. Dieser Entwurf wird sowohl auf die technische als auch auf die 
wirtschaftliche Machbarkeit untersucht. 
In Teil 1 wurde das bestehendes Model des zuvor vorgeschlagenen Energiespeichersystems durch 
das Modell eines Biomassevergasers erweitert. Der Einfluss der Menge an Synthesegas, welche 
dem System von dem Biomassevergaser zugeführt wird, wurde untersucht. Anschließend wurde 
eine konsistente Definition des Wirkungsgrades des hybriden Energiespeichersystems für die 
verschiedenen Betriebsmodi eingeführt. 
Die 
Ergebnisse 
zeigen, 
dass 
der 
Betrieb 
der 
Anlage 
durch 
Variation 
des 
Synthesegasmassenstromes so beeinflusst werden kann, dass er dem Lastprofil, welches durch die 
Nachfrage des Stromnetzes und daher für einen wirtschaftlich optimalen Betrieb erwünscht ist, 
entspricht. Mit Wirkungsgraden zwischen 86 % und 89 % ermöglicht das System zusätzlich eine 
sehr effiziente Veredelung von Synthesegas zu synthetischem Erdgas. Ein Vergleich zu 
konventioneller Veredelung von Biosynthesegas über Wasserelektrolyse, welche einen 
Wirkungsgrad von maximal 78 % erreicht, zeigt, dass die Anwendung eines solchen Systems 
interessant sein könnte. 
Für den Entwurf eines integrierten Wärmetauschers wurde ein detailliertes Modell in der Software 
EES erstellt. Der neue Entwurf wurde auf zwei unterschiedliche Systeme angewandt, nämlich auf 
ein System, welches eine endotherme katalytische Reaktion innerhalb des Wärmetauschers 
benötigt und auf das System aus Teil 1 dieser Arbeit, in welchem diese nicht benötigt wird. 
Die Resultate zeigen, dass der Wärmetauscher am besten für die Verwendung in einem System 
ohne katalytische Reaktion und Temperaturdifferenz von maximal 100 °C innerhalb des 
Wärmetauschers geeignet ist. 
Der neue Entwurf wurde mit einem Vergleichssystem, bestehend aus einem kommerziellen 
Druckbehälter und einem Plattenwärmetauscher verglichen. Der Vergleich wurde anhand der Masse 
des zu verwendenden Stahls durchgeführt, da diese als etwa proportional zu den gesamten 
Materialkosten angenommen werden kann. Die Ergebnisse zeigen, dass der neue Entwurf des 
Wärmetauschers bezogen auf die Materialkosten wirtschaftlich kaum mit dem Vergleichssystem 
konkurrieren kann. Da jedoch der Plattenwärmetauscher im Vergleichssystem für derart hohe 
Drücke und Temperaturen auch speziell angefertigt werden muss, könnte der neue Entwurf aufgrund 
des nicht allzu großen Massenunterschiedes interessant sein. Dies sollte durch eine detaillierte 
Wirtschaflichkeitsanalyse untersucht werden. 
 
==PAGE:8==
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:9==
Preface 
vi 
Preface 
This Master Thesis is part of my studies in the master’s programme ‘Energie- und Verfahrenstechnik’ 
at Technische Universität Berlin, TU Berlin. The work was carried out at the Technical University of 
Denmark, DTU, under the supervision of Lasse Røngaard Clausen. Supervising professor at TU 
Berlin was Prof. George Tsatsaronis. 
The work is divided into two different parts. In Part I a hybrid electricity storage system using 
pressurized reversible solid oxide cells (SOC) and biomass gasification is presented. In Part II an 
integrated SOC stack in a heat exchanger for use in the system described in Part I of the work, or 
other systems using pressurized solid oxide cells, is designed and analysed. 
Each part is structured into an abstract, a main body including introduction, methods, results, 
discussion and conclusion and an appendix. 
 
The overall aim of the work was firstly to extend a previously described energy storage system based 
on reversible solid oxide cells with a biomass gasifier unit. In this way the system can be operated 
more flexibly and it is possible to efficiently store electric energy and produce synthetic natural gas, 
which can be delivered into the natural gas system. Secondly, an innovative pressure bearing, 
recuperative heat exchanger placed around the solid oxide cell should be designed and assessed 
for technical and economic feasibility. 
 
For the assessment of the first task an existing DNA model of the previously described system was 
extended and the influence of the amount of syngas added to the system was analysed. A consistent 
method to define the efficiency of the hybrid energy storage system in different operation modes was 
presented. 
It was found that by changing the syngas mass flow, the operation of the system can be optimized 
to work according to the necessities of the electricity grid and/or the electricity price. In addition to 
the efficient energy storage, the system provides a very efficient syngas upgrading to synthetic 
natural gas with efficiencies of 86 % and 89 %, depending on the input syngas mass flow. This is 
significantly higher than the efficiency of maximum 78 % achieved by conventional bio-syngas 
upgrading through water electrolysis. 
 
For the new design of the integrated heat exchanger a detailed model of the heat exchanger was 
developed in EES software. The new design was evaluated for two different systems. Firstly, 
including an endothermic catalytic reaction in the cold gas of the heat exchanger, which is required 
for previously proposed electricity storage systems based on pressurized solid oxide cells. Secondly, 
for the system proposed in Part I of this work. In this system, no catalytic reaction is required within 
the heat exchanger. 
It was found that the system is technically most feasible for the case without reaction and with a 
minimum temperature difference of 100 °C and additional water cooling of the pressure bearing 
steel. 
A comparison of the integrated heat exchanger design with a reference case consisting of a 
commercial pressure vessel and a plate heat exchanger based on material mass as an indicator for 
cost of material was carried out. It revealed that the economic performance of the integrated heat 
exchanger design is disadvantageous compared to the reference case. However, the high 
temperature and pressure of the systems require a specially designed plate heat exchanger. The 
difference in material mass for the optimal integrated heat exchanger configuration is small and thus 
a more detailed economic evaluation might find that the integrated design is actually competitive.

Part I 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Hybrid electricity storage system 
based on pressurized solid oxide cells 
and biomass gasification 
 
 
==PAGE:14==
 
 
 
==PAGE:15==
Abstract 
i 
 
Abstract 
A future fossil-free energy system based on intermittent renewable energy sources will require 
efficient and flexible large-scale electrical energy storage (EES) systems, as well as alternative 
solutions for the heating and transport sector.  
Electrical energy storage (EES) systems using reversible solid oxide cells (ReSOCs) have 
shown to be promising solutions for large-scale energy storage, in terms of cost and efficiency. 
The combination of pressurized ReSOCs and catalytic reactors is a particularly interesting op-
tion, enabling the production of synthetic natural gas (SNG) during the electrolysis mode and 
allowing the use of the existing natural gas (NG) infrastructures for the SNG storage. Addition-
ally this solution achieves roundtrip efficiencies with values up to 80%. 
In this work a hybrid EES system using pressurized solid oxide cells and a two-stage biomass 
gasifier is analysed. By using the syngas produced by a two-stage gasifier in both operating 
modes, the system has the ability to produce extra SNG, which is not reused in fuel cell mode, 
and can be used instead for transportation or heating purposes. 
Through the new system configuration, the operating pressure and the inlet temperature to the 
SOC become independent and it is possible to operate the SOC with similar inlet temperatures 
as the operation temperatures. This is advantageous because it decreases the thermo-me-
chanical stress on the SOC. 
The results showed that an operation with a syngas mass flow of 4.5 kg/s is most promising. 
Firstly, the inlet temperatures to the SOC of 670 °C in fuel cell- and 681 °C in electrolysis 
operation mode are beneficial for a long-time operation of the SOC. Secondly, the capacity 
factor of 20 % in fuel cell and 80 % in electrolysis operating mode fit the expected future storage 
necessity of 15-20 % of the average load. This allows theoretically for a non-stop operation of 
the system, which increases the economic feasibility of the system. 
The overall syngas to SNG upgrading efficiency of the hybrid EES is found to be between 86.9 
% and 88.1 % for different input syngas mass flows, which is higher than for conventional bio-
syngas upgrading with a maximum upgrading efficiency of 78 %.  
The results show that the hybrid EES system introduces the possibility of producing SNG and 
storing electricity efficiently and introduces the possibility to tune the operation in order to op-
erate non-stop and to maximize the revenue of the system.

==PAGE:25==
1   Introduction 
1 
1 Introduction 
In November 2017, the UN Climate Change Conference took place in Bonn, Germany [1]. The 
participating countries consolidated the Paris agreement, which aims to reduce the global 
greenhouse gas emissions with the goal of keeping the increase of the global average tem-
perature below 2°C above pre-industrial levels [2]. 
The European Union aims to reach these goals, by reducing the greenhouse gas emissions to 
20% and 40%, increasing the share of renewable energy sources to 20% and 27% and in-
creasing the energy efficiency to 20% and 27% compared to 1990 levels until 2020 and 2030 
respectively [3], [4]. 
The Danish government established more ambitious goals, aiming for a share of 50% of the 
national energy demand covert by renewable energy sources by 2030. By 2050, Denmark 
aims to be independent from coal, oil and natural gas [5]. 
To reach these goals, the utilization of intermittent renewable sources as solar and wind will 
increase. In order to balance the mismatches between these and the fluctuating power de-
mand, energy storage systems are required. The future storage need in the US and in Europe 
has been estimated to be 15-20% of the annual average load, i.e. two to three months of 
storage [6], [7].  
However, not only the electricity sector, but also the heat and transport sector will change in 
order to become fossil free. Hence, additional flexible and adaptive systems, providing heat, 
biofuels etc. are required to achieve an effective energy system [8], [9]. 
1.1 Electrical energy storage technologies 
There are different technologies for electrical energy storage (EES). Factors to evaluate and 
compare these technologies are mainly capacity, cost and efficiency, but also minor properties 
as safety, reliability and durability [10]. Especially for storage durations of several months, 
many existing electrical energy storage systems have problems. The main reasons are low 
efficiency, high cost and the large scale involved [11]. 
The only system fulfilling the required properties of high capacity, high efficiency and relatively 
low capital cost per unit of energy is pumped hydroelectric storage. It works by pumping water 
into a higher reservoir in times of low electricity demand. At times of high electricity demand, 
water is returned to the lower reservoir, driving a turbine to generate electricity. The storage 
efficiency, including conversion and evaporation losses, is 70 - 85 %. The main disadvantage 
of pumped hydroelectric storage is the necessity of suitable geographic circumstances [12], 
[13]. 
The only other commercially available technology, providing storage capacities above 100 
MW, is compressed air energy storage. In this technology, air is compressed and stored in an 
underground cavern. When electricity is demanded, the air is heated up and expanded in gas 
turbines. Usually the reheating is done with fossil fuels, mainly natural gas, which makes the 
system less attractive for a fossil-free energy system. Thus, new compressed air energy stor-
age systems are investigated, e.g. using additional thermal storages which use renewable 
energy sources to heat up the storage [12]. 
Batteries are ideally suited for EES applications, as they provide fuel flexibility and offer im-
portant operating benefits to the electricity utility. With 65 – 90 %, they have a high energy 
efficiency and they can react rapidly to load changes and enhance the system stability. How-
ever, batteries are not suitable for large-scale EES, due to the low energy density, small power 
==PAGE:26==
1.2   Solid oxide cells 
2 
capacity, high maintenance costs, short cycle life and limited discharge capability [12]. Detailed 
descriptions of different battery types and their properties can be found in [10], [12]–[14].  
Some other EES technologies, e.g. flow batteries, flywheels or thermal energy storage, exist 
but will not be explained in the current work. Further information about the working principles 
and the ad- and disadvantages of these technologies can be found in [10], [12]–[14]. 
 
Reversible utilization of fuel cells is another interesting way to store large amounts of electrical 
energy. There are different ways of using fuel cells for this purpose. In hydrogen fuel cells, 
hydrogen and oxygen are used to produce electricity and water. This process can be reversed 
in an electrolysis cell to produce hydrogen, in case of low electricity demand. For this applica-
tion, different fuel cell types, as Proton Exchange Membrane Fuel Cells (PEMFCs), Alkaline 
Fuel Cells (AFCs) or Phosphoric Acid Fuel Cells (PAFCs) can be used. Advantages of these 
systems are, among others, high energy density and ability of implementing different scales. 
However, relatively high costs and low round-trip efficiencies of 20-50% are disadvantageous 
[12]. Additionally, the hydrogen storage technologies projected up to now are facing problems 
like low ratios of stored hydrogen per unit weight, usage of seldom earth materials or low stor-
age efficiency due to high energy consumption for compressors or maintaining specific tem-
peratures [13]. 
The problems, connected with the hydrogen storage can be avoided by using different types 
of fuel cells. Direct-methanol fuel cells (DMFCs) are a subcategory of PEMFCs using methanol 
as direct fuel. The storage of methanol is much easier and requires less space than the storage 
of hydrogen. However, the efficiency of DMFCs is low and the crossover of methanol and water 
across the membrane leads to problems [12], [15].  
All of the above mentioned fuel cells work at temperatures lower than 220 °C [15]. Other types 
of fuel cells work at higher temperatures, which enables an internal reforming of hydrocarbons 
to generate hydrogen within the fuel cell. Molten Carbonate Fuel Cells (MCFCs) work at tem-
peratures around 650 °C and use either molten lithium potassium or lithium sodium carbonate 
salt as the electrolyte. Advantages of this technology are the avoiding of carbon monoxide 
poisoning due to the high temperatures, the substitution of platinum catalysts by less expen-
sive nickel species and the possibility of using excess heat for a combined heat and power 
plant. The high power generating efficiency makes them attractive for large-scale industrial 
processes and electricity generating turbines. For MCFCs, the elevated temperatures lead also 
to some problems, such as longer start-up periods, unsuitability for transport and the temper-
ature and corrosive nature of electrolyte makes them unsafe for home power generation [12]. 
The usage of reversible solid oxide cells (ReSOCs) is especially promising, because it has the 
advantages of MCFCs but with higher stability and fuel flexibility. In this work, a system using 
ReSOCs is examined. In the next sections, the working principle of SOCs and previous works 
on systems using them are presented. 
1.2 Solid oxide cells 
As implied by the name, the main component of EES systems based on ReSOC are solid 
oxide cells. ReSOCs operate in two different operating modes. In electrolysis mode, electricity 
is used to convert a low energy gas, consisting of mainly H2O and/or CO2, to a high energy 
gas, consisting of H2, CO and/or CH4. In fuel cell mode, the process is reversed and electricity 
is generated by converting the high energy to low energy gas. 
In Figure 1.1, a schematic of a planar SOC is depicted. It can be seen that it consists of a 
positive electrode (brown layer), an electrolyte (purple layer) and a negative electrode (green 
==PAGE:27==
1   Introduction 
3 
layer). The three components are usually summarized as PEN. The PEN is made of ceramic 
materials. This insures stability at operating temperatures of 600-1000 °C. In SOCs also the 
electrolyte is solid, which is not the case in some other fuel cell types. The electrolyte functions 
as a conductor of oxide ions. Usually the thin electrolyte is made of yttrium stabilized zirconia 
(YSZ). YSZ shows good performances at temperatures above 700 °C [15]. For intermediate 
temperatures of 600-700 °C, relevant for EES systems, other types of electrolytes are used. 
Investigations showed that strontium- and magnesium-doped lanthanum gallate (LSGM) elec-
trolyte are especially suitable for such applications [11], [16] A good overview of different elec-
trolyte materials suited for low-temperature applications is provided in [17]. 
 
Figure 1.1 Schematic of a planar SOC [18] 
The LSGM is sandwiched between two porous electrodes. Due to the reversible operation of 
the SOC, it is disadvantageous to call them positive and negative electrode or cathode and 
anode. Instead, the nomenclature shown in Figure 1.2 is used. The fuel electrode denotes the 
electrode, which is in contact with the fuel gas, i.e. the anode in SOFC mode and the cathode 
in SOEC mode. The oxygen (or air) electrode is the other electrode, which is in contact with 
the air, i.e. the cathode in SOFC mode and the anode in SOEC mode. The air is necessary in 
SOFC mode in order to supply the oxygen ions for the reactions at the fuel electrode. In SOEC 
mode it is used as a sweep gas in order to enhance the performance of the cell [16]. The same 
nomenclature is used for the fuel and oxygen (or air) channel. For applications at intermediate 
temperatures the fuel electrode is usually made of nickel and LSGM. For the oxygen electrode, 
lanthanum strontium cobalt ferrite (LSCF) is used [15], [16].  
The gases entering the SOC are oxidized or reduced at the triple phase boundary (TPB). The 
TBP describes the zone where the gas phase, the electrode and the electrolyte meet [15]. The 
gases have to diffuse through the porous electrode to react at the TPB. 
The reaction at the oxygen electrode in SOFC mode is always

==PAGE:29==
1   Introduction 
5 
Bierschenk et al. conducted experiments showing that at ambient pressure the outlet compo-
sition of the gases is nearly equal to the thermodynamic equilibrium composition [19], [21]. 
This means that the kinetics within the SOC are fast. In the same paper it was shown that 
increased pressure (~ 10 bar) and/or reduced temperature (~ 600 °C) are advantageous for 
the CH4-production. Jensen et al. showed experimentally that also at elevated pressures (18.7 
bar) the exit composition corresponds to the equilibrium composition [22]. 
The CH4-production of equation (1.6) is important for the thermal behaviour of the cell, because 
it has a high heat of reaction with the opposite sign of reactions (1.3) and (1.5). In SOFC mode 
this leads to a less endothermic reaction and thus the required airflow can be reduced. In 
SOEC mode it leads to a less endothermic reaction, which is beneficial for the efficiency of the 
SOC [16]. 
A system based on ReSOC does not only consist of a SOC stack. It requires additional com-
ponents, e.g. to heat/cool and compress/expand the gas flows or condensers or separators to 
remove liquids. These components are usually summarized as balance-of-plant (BOP). They 
can have a big influence on the efficiency of the EES system. Hence, the operating conditions 
of the SOC have to be optimized in order to find a high performance of the SOC and a reason-
able power consumption of the BOP. 
1.3 Systems based on reversible solid oxide cells 
In recent years, different works were published emphasising on the usability of reversible solid 
oxide cell systems for electrical energy storage. ReSOCs can work as solid oxide electrolysis 
cell (SOEC), converting gases with low energy content and electricity to a gas with high energy 
content and as solid oxide fuel cell (SOFC) with the reversed purpose. 
In 2011, Bierschenk et al. proposed the usage of ReSOCs for EES. It was shown that it is 
possible to have methane production within a SOEC. This has a positive effect on the efficiency 
of the SOC stack because the CH4-forming reactions require less heat than H2- and CO-form-
ing reactions. The behaviour of a ReSOC, using fuel cycles between H2O-CO2-rich and CH4-
H2-rich gases showed, that increased pressure (~ 10 bar) and reduced temperature (~ 600 °C) 
are advantageous for the CH4-production [21]. 
Jensen et al. proposed a large-scale EES system (MWh-GWh) based on ReSOCs, using two 
caverns, one to store the CO2-H2O-rich “exhaust gas” and the one for a CH4-H2-CO-rich “fuel 
gas”. An economic analysis of the system was conducted, showing that the storage cost can 
be estimated to ca. 3 ¢/(kW h), which is comparable to pumped hydroelectric storage [11] 
Wendel et al. built a thermodynamic model for the system proposed by Jensen et al, including 
the additional heat and power requirements, provided by the balance of plant hardware. The 
optimal operating conditions were found at elevated stack pressure (20 bar) and intermediate 
temperature (680 °C). The round-trip (RT) efficiency at optimal operation is above 72 % [23]. 
The round-trip efficiency 𝜂RT denotes the storage efficiency and is defined as 
 
𝜂RT = 𝑊̇ SOFC,net
𝑊̇ SOEC,net
= |𝑊̇ SOFC + 𝑊̇ SOFC,BOP|
|𝑊̇ SOEC + 𝑊̇ SOEC,BOP| 
(1.7) 
 
𝑊̇  denotes electrical power, which is defined positive in case of input and negative for output. 
The subscripts SOFC and SOEC denote the values of the cell in fuel cell and electrolysis mode, 
respectively. The subscripts SOFC, net and SOEC, 𝑛𝑒𝑡 denote the net power in- or output of the 
whole system and SOFC, BOP and SOEC, 𝐵𝑂𝑃 the power in- or output of the balance-of-plant. 
==PAGE:30==
1.3   Systems based on reversible solid oxide cells 
6 
Wendel et al. proposed also a small-scale EES system for distributed energy storage applica-
tions (100 kW/ 800 kWh). Two different systems were examined, one storing vapour in the 
storage tanks and one using water separation. The round-trip efficiency of the “stored vapour” 
system is higher with 73.7 % compared to the efficiency of 68.3 % for the “water separation” 
system. Additionally the storage cost was calculated to be 8 ¢/(kW h) and 7 ¢/(kW h), respec-
tively. [24] 
Monti et al. studied a system of the same size (100 kW) and introduced a methanator after the 
SOEC in electrolysis mode. This leads to a higher methane content in the CH4 rich gas and 
thus to a higher energy density. The round-trip efficiency of this system, working at ambient 
pressure, is 68.9 % including water separation [25]. 
Butera et al. presented a new design of a large scale EES (~90 MW), using pressurized 
ReSOC and catalytic reactors. In electrolysis mode, catalytic reactors are used after the SOEC 
to produce gas-grid-quality synthetic natural gas (SNG). This leads to the possibility of storing 
electricity in the existing gas grid. In fuel cell mode, gas is taken from the natural gas grid and 
oxidized in the fuel cell to generate electricity. Thereby, CO2-rich gas is produced, which is 
stored in a storage tank or a cavern and later used again in electrolysis mode. The round-trip 
efficiency is 80 % and is thus comparable to pumped hydroelectric storage. In Appendix B, the 
flowsheet of the system in electrolysis and fuel cell mode are depicted [26], [27]. 
All of these EES, using ReSOCs are based on storage tanks, storing the CO2-rich gas and, 
except of the last system, also the CH4-rich gas. This leads to a connection between the elec-
trolysis and the fuel cell mode, meaning that the operating time in both modes is equal. As the 
storage need is expected to be 15-20% of the annual load, the EES system can work continu-
ously over a year. This means that in some parts of the year no revenue is earned with this 
system, leading to economic disadvantages. In this time, the system is either shut down or 
kept in standby. 
Shutting down the system leads to a low reactivity to load changes because the high operating 
temperature of SOC systems induces long start-up periods. Alternatively, the system is kept 
in standby, which is realized by keeping the SOC at its operation temperature. This is done by 
compensating the heat losses with a current flow. This leads to constant expenses, which 
diminishes the profitability of the system. However, for large-scale systems, the heat losses 
become small compared to the power input/output, as the heat loss scales with the surface 
area, while the power scales with the volume. Hence, for large-scale systems this is a minor 
problem. 
 
Sigurjonsson and Clausen proposed an alternative polygeneration plant, using ambient pres-
sure ReSOCs and biomass gasification producing electrofuel, heat and power [28]. The idea 
is to produce bio-SNG from biomass, when electricity demand is low. This is done by generat-
ing syngas in a biomass gasifier and hydrogen in the SOEC. Both gases react in a methane 
reactor, producing a gas-grid-quality SNG. When electricity demand is high, syngas from the 
biomass gasifier is used in the SOFC and an additional gas turbine to generate electricity. 
Thus, the bio-SNG produced in SOEC mode is not used in SOFC mode, but can be used for 
transportation or heating. This decouples the operating modes from each other, allowing a 
continuous operation of the system, depending on the demand of the electricity grid. Addition-
ally, the system has a surplus of heat, which can be used to provide district heating. An eco-
nomic analysis of the system showed that the revenues of the district heating have a major 
influence on the economic feasibility of the system. 
 
Within this work, the design proposals of Butera et al. and Sigurjonsson and Clausen are com-
bined. A hybrid electricity storage system, using pressurized reversible solid oxide cells and 
==PAGE:31==
1   Introduction 
7 
biomass gasification is designed and evaluated. This system allows for storing electrical en-
ergy in the natural gas grid, by producing synthetic natural gas (SNG) during electrolysis mode 
and the reversed operation in fuel cell mode. By using the syngas produced by the gasifier in 
both operating modes, the system has the ability to produce additional SNG, which is not re-
used in fuel cell mode, but can be used in transportation or heating. 
 
The system design is presented and the crucial components of the system are explained in 
chapter 2. In chapter 3, the system is evaluated for an operation pressure of 20 bar. Thereby 
the variation of the ratio between syngas and SNG in fuel cell mode is examined and the most 
suitable operating condition is determined. By introducing consistent definitions of energy effi-
ciencies, the system is divided into subsystems and further analysed. The subsystems includ-
ing syngas upgrading are then compared to conventional bio-syngas upgrading. Additionally, 
alternative operations of the system and their advantages are explained shortly. In chapter 4, 
the results of the work are summarized shortly and an outlook to further interesting works in 
future is given.

2 Hybrid electrical energy storage system using solid oxide

The new design of a hybrid EES system using pressurized SOCs and a biomass gasifier is 
based on the design of Butera [26], [27]. The main difference between the two systems is that 
in the new design, syngas from a two-stage biomass gasifier is added to the natural gas and 
the CO2-rich gas in the fuel cell and electrolysis mode, respectively. The detailed flowsheets 
of the previous work of Butera are shown in Appendix B. Figure 2.1 shows a simplified flow-
sheet of the new design of the EES system, while detailed flowsheets are shown in Appendix 
A. 
In section 2.1, the software “Dynamic Network Analysis” (DNA) used for the calculations of the 
system is introduced briefly. In section 2.2 and 2.3, the system design in fuel cell and electrol-
ysis operating mode are explained, respectively. In section 2.4, the most important compo-
nents are explained and the analogies and differences to the previous system of Butera are 
shown. The numbers of components in Figure 2.1 refer to the subsection of section 2.4, in 
which they are described.

2.1   Software and modelling 
10 
2.1 Software and modelling 
The system was modelled in the in-house modelling tool “Dynamic Network Analysis” (DNA). 
DNA is a component based simulation tool for zero-dimensional thermodynamic modelling 
[29], [30]. 
The model used in this work is based on the model of Butera. Therefore, the reader is referred 
to [26] and [27] for a detailed description of the model and the underlying equations. As in the 
work of Butera, the model of the EES system is divided into two separate models, one for the 
fuel cell and one for the electrolysis operating mode. This allows for a lighter model with easier 
convergence. The two models are connected to each other by ensuring the inlet gas compo-
sition of one mode corresponds to the outlet composition of the other mode. 
In the following sections, the system design of, and the operation in, fuel cell and electrolysis 
operating mode are explained. The definitions and equations, necessary for the understanding 
of this work are shown in section 2.4. 
2.2 Fuel cell operating mode 
In this mode the system converts a mixture of natural gas (NG) and syngas from a biomass 
gasifier to a CO2-rich gas while generating electricity The CO2 rich gas is stored. A simplified 
flow sheet of the hybrid EES system in fuel cell operating mode is provided in Figure 2.2. In 
Appendix A, a detailed flowsheet can be found.  
 
Figure 2.2 Simplified flowsheet of the hybrid EES system in fuel cell mode

==PAGE:35==
2   Hybrid electrical energy storage system using solid oxide cells and biomass gasification 
11 
Starting from the bottom left corner, natural gas is taken from the NG grid. The gas grid works 
at elevated pressures. Thus, the NG is expanded to operation pressure of the system using 
turbines. Recuperative heat exchangers are used to increase the temperature of the natural 
gas for the expansion. Afterwards, NG is mixed with syngas coming from a two-stage biomass 
gasifier.  
The mix of NG and syngas enters the humidifier, where preheated water is used to increase 
the steam content of the gas. The water is preheated in the condenser used to condensate the 
water from the CO2-rich gas before is exits the system. The humidified gas exiting the humid-
ifier is now heated, using the hot CO2-rich gas coming from the SOFC. If the required inlet 
temperature to the SOFC cannot be reached with the recuperator, due to restrictions of a min-
imum temperature difference in the heat exchanger, the temperature can be further increased 
in an adiabatic methanation reactor (methanator). If this is not required, the adiabatic methana-
tor is bypassed in order to avoid the pressure loss within the component. 
Afterwards, the gas mixture enters the SOFC, where it is oxidized according to the reactions 
shown in chapter 1.2, while generating electricity. The exiting CO2-rich gas is then cooled in 
the recuperator and most of the steam in the gas is condensed in the condenser. The remain-
ing steam is removed using a water separator. Afterwards the CO2-rich gas is compressed and 
cooled to storage pressure and temperature before entering the storage tank or cavern. 
2.3 Electrolysis operating mode 
During the electricity-consuming mode, called electrolysis operating mode, electricity is used 
to convert CO2-rich gas and syngas from a biomass gasifier to synthetic natural gas (SNG), 
which is injected to the natural gas grid. In this mode, the SOC works as an SOEC. A simplified 
flow sheet of the hybrid EES system in electrolysis operating mode is provided in Figure 2.3. 
In Appendix A, a detailed flowsheet can be found. 
Starting from the bottom left corner, CO2-rich gas is taken from a storage tank or cavern and 
is expanded to operation pressure of the system, while using recuperated heat. Then it is mixed 
with the syngas coming from a two-stage biomass gasifier and the mixture is then split into two 
streams. One is used to increase the CO2-content of the CH4-rich gas before entering the 
cooled methanator. The other stream goes to the humidifier, where the steam content is in-
creased by using preheated liquid water. Afterwards, the steam content is further increased by 
mixing the gas with steam, generated by cooling the cooled methanator. The humidified gas 
stream enters now the recuperator where it is preheated using the heat from the hot CH4-rich 
gas coming from the SOEC. The preheated gas goes to the adiabatic methanator, where the 
temperature is further increased to the required SOEC inlet temperature, while increasing the 
methane content in the gas. 
The gas mixture enters the SOEC, where it is reduced according to the reactions shown in 
chapter 1.2, while consuming electricity. The CH4-rich gas exiting the SOEC is then cooled in 
the recuperator and mixed with the dry mixture of CO2-rich gas and syngas, coming from the 
gas splitter. This mixture enters the cooled methanator, where the methane content is in-
creased in order to be usable in the NG grid. Finally, the steam content is reduced in the 
condenser and water-removal system and the SNG is compressed and cooled to the pressure 
and temperature of the NG grid. 
==PAGE:36==
2.4   Important components of the system 
12 
 
Figure 2.3 Simplified flowsheet of the hybrid EES system in electrolysis mode 
2.4 Important components of the system 
2.4.1 Humidifier and condenser 
The humidifier is used in both operating modes to increase the steam content of the gas going 
to the SOC. This is necessary for increasing the H/C-ratio, which avoids carbon formation 
within the SOC (see section 2.4.7). The water in the system is mainly recycled between the 
condenser and the humidifier.  
As shown in the detailed flow sheets in Appendix A, the condenser is divided into a high-
temperature (HT) and a low-temperature (LT) condenser. The HT-condenser is used to con-
dense a part of the steam of the gas exiting the system, and the hot water is then used in the 
humidifier. The LT condenser does not provide water for the humidifier and is only used to 
decrease the steam content in the gas. 
In contrast to the system of Butera, the HT condenser cannot provide the required heat for the 
humidifier. Hence, an additional recuperative heat exchanger is used to preheat the water be-
fore entering the humidifier. The additional heat exchanger can be found in Figure A.1 as HEX3 
and in Figure A.2 as HEX6.

2.4.2 Gas splitter 
The gas splitter is only used in electrolysis operating mode. A part of the gas bypasses the 
SOEC and is mixed with the CH4-rich gas coming from the SOEC. This is required to increase 
carbon content of gas going to cooled methanator in order to convert the excess hydrogen 
from the SOEC to methane. 
There are different locations in the system, where the gas splitter can be placed. The simplest 
ones are after the mixing of syngas from the gasifier and the CO2-rich gas (as shown in in the 
Figure 2.3) or before the mixing. Preliminary showed that by placing the splitter after the mixer 
enables the operation of the system for all mass flow ratios between the syngas and CO2-rich 
gas. The other case does not work for small mass flows of the CO2-rich gas, because of the 
insufficient CO2-supply for the cooled methanator.  
Other configurations, as a splitter before the mixer with an additional splitter for the syngas 
stream, enabling to return some of the syngas in case of necessity, could be used. However, 
in this work the configuration shown in Figure 2.3 with the splitter after the mixer is used. 
2.4.3 Recuperator 
The recuperator is a heat exchanger used to preheat gas before the SOC, using the hot gas 
exiting the SOC. In system of Butera, a catalytic recuperator was needed in fuel cell mode for 
steam reforming, in order to increase the H2 and CO ratio of the gas before the SOFC. This is 
done, to ensure that the inlet composition is close to chemical equilibrium, which is important 
for thermal management of the SOFC [26]. In the new system design, steam reforming can be 
avoided adding the syngas from the biomass gasifier. Through this, a composition near to 
chemical equilibrium can be achieved at the inlet of the SOFC. 
Avoiding the catalytic steam reforming within the recuperator brings some advantages to the 
system. The steam reforming reaction is endothermic. This means that the hot gas coming 
from the SOFC has to provide the heat for the reaction, in addition to the heat for increasing 
the inlet temperature to the SOFC. Hence, by avoiding the catalytic reaction, the size of the 
recuperator can be diminished. This is especially important for the design of the recuperator, 
which is the topic of Part II of this work. 
The lower heat requirements by avoiding catalytic steam reforming lead also to the possibility 
of the introduction of a high temperature heat output from the system. In Figure A.1, the com-
ponent HEX5 is shown in the detailed flowsheet. This heat exchanger is used to preheat the 
water going to the humidifier and additionally provides a high temperature heat output, which 
could be used as process steam or to boost the electricity production with an additional steam 
or ORC cycle. 
Additionally, the reactions in the catalytic recuperator have a strong influence on the system 
operation. Butera et al. showed, that due to the catalytic reaction in the recuperator, the oper-
ation pressure and the inlet temperature to the SOFC are dependent from each other. It was 
shown that an increase of the inlet temperature to the SOFC from 600°C to 610°C leads to an 
increase of the operating pressure from 19.39 bar to 27.28 bar [26]. Due to the strongly in-
creasing pressure with increasing inlet temperatures it was not convenient to go to higher inlet 
temperatures than 600°C. 
By avoiding the catalytic recuperator in the new systems, the inlet temperature to the SOFC 
and the operating pressure become independent and can be varied. Hence, higher inlet tem-
perature to the SOFC are feasible at every pressure. This is advantageous for the operation 
of the SOC, because an inlet temperature similar to the operation temperature of the SOC 
stack decreases the thermo-mechanical stress in the SOC and insures a more uniform tem-
perature profile along the gas channels. Additionally, catalytic recuperators are not standard 
==PAGE:38==
2.4   Important components of the system 
14 
industrial components. Hence, the usage of a standard heat exchanger decreases the invest-
ment costs of the system. 
2.4.4 Cooled methanator 
The cooled methanator is used in electrolysis mode to increase the methane content of the 
SNG after the SOEC. The methanator is cooled with water, which is evaporated and afterwards 
mixed with the gas before going to the SOEC. This is required to increase the H/C ratio. 
The reactions taking place in the cooled methanator are shown in Eq. (2.1) and (2.2). 
 
3𝐻2 + 𝐶𝑂↔𝐶𝐻4 + 𝐻2𝑂 
(2.1) 
4𝐻2 + 𝐶𝑂2 ↔𝐶𝐻4 + 2𝐻2𝑂 
(2.2) 
 
From these reaction equations it can be extracted that the methane production in the methana-
tor can be maximized by setting the gas module 𝑀= 3. The gas module is defined as  
 
𝑀= 𝐻2 −𝐶𝑂
𝐶𝑂+ 𝐶𝑂2
 
(2.3) 
 
Hence, the amount of gas bypassing the SOEC via the gas splitter is set in order to achieve 
𝑀= 3 at the inlet to the cooled methanator. 
2.4.5 Adiabatic methanator 
As in the system of Butera, the adiabatic methanator is used in electrolysis mode to increase 
the inlet temperature to the SOEC. In the new hybrid system design, it can also be used in fuel 
cell mode to increase the inlet temperature further than the limits, implied by the minimal tem-
perature difference in the recuperator. If this is not needed, it is bypassed, using a three-way 
valve. 
2.4.6 Two-stage biomass gasifier 
In both operating modes, a two-stage biomass gasifier developed at DTU is used [31], [32]. 
The advantages of this design of a gasifier is the high cold gas efficiency and the very low tar 
content in the produced syngas.  
The two-stage gasifier separates the pyrolysis and the gasification reactions in two compo-
nents. In the first stage, the biomass is pyrolyzed in a screw conveyer. The conveyer is heated 
by passing the syngas from the gasifier on the outside of the closed conveyer. Then, the py-
rolysis is partially oxidized before passing through a downdraft fixed bed. In the fixed bed, the 
gasification reactions take place. For large scale applications, the use of other reactor types, 
i.e. updraft fixed bed or fluidized bed reactors, is considered. 
In this work, the gasifier is not included in the model in order to have a lighter model, in which 
convergence is achieved more easily. For the syngas composition, the results of Clausen et 
al. shown in Table 2.1 are used [33]. This composition is for an air-blown two-stage gasifier, 
working with wet wood chips at atmospheric pressure. The wood chips have a water content 
of 42.5 %. 
 
 
==PAGE:39==
2   Hybrid electrical energy storage system using solid oxide cells and biomass gasification 
15 
Table 2.1 Dry and nitrogen-free molar composition of syngas, coming from an air-blown two-stage gasifier work-
ing with a feed of wet wood chips (42.5% water) at atmospheric pressure [33] 
𝒙𝑯𝟐 [%] 
𝒙𝑪𝑶 [%] 
𝒙𝑪𝑶𝟐 [%] 
𝒙𝑪𝑯𝟒 [%] 
48.3 
32.8 
17.7 
1.2 
 
Within the hybrid EES system, an oxygen-blown gasifier is used because the nitrogen from the 
air would lead to an SNG composition, which cannot be used in the NG-grid. The composition 
in Table 2.1 is used in this work, even though the system works at elevated pressures and is 
oxygen-blown. The molar ratios of the H2, CO and CO2 are only depending on the water-gas 
shift reaction. In Eq. (1.5), it can be seen that the shift reaction is not pressure dependent, 
according to Le Chatelier’s principle. The CH4-content is very small compared to the molar 
ratio of the other components. All of the methane is produced within the pyrolysis reactor, while 
part of it is oxidized during the partial oxidation. The reactions during pyrolysis are not pressure 
dependent, leading to a constant CH4-content. 
The two-stage gasifier used in [33] is an updated design of the one presented in [31], [32]. In 
the updated version, the wood chips are dried in a steam drier before entering the pyrolysis 
reactor. This leads to the requirement of sufficient heat output of the EES system to provide 
heat for the drying. 
Within this work it is wanted to have the biomass gasifier working non-stop in full-load. In order 
to add this aspect to the simulation of the hybrid EES system, the syngas mass flow 𝑚̇ Syn 
entering the system is set equal for both operating modes: 
 
𝑚̇ Syn,SOFC = 𝑚̇ Syn,SOEC = 𝑚̇ Syn 
(2.4) 
2.4.7 Solid oxide cell 
The working principle and the reactions within the solid oxide cell have been explained in chap-
ter 1.2. In this section, the relevant parameters for the operation of the solid oxide cell, used in 
this work, are explained. 
The power input of the SOC stack during electrolysis mode and the power output in fuel cell 
mode are calculated with 
 
𝑊̇ SOC = −𝑉SOC ⋅𝐼SOC 
(2.5) 
 
𝐼SOC denotes the total electric current in the SOC. In electrolysis mode the current is negative 
and in fuel cell mode it is positive. The minus sign in the equation is used to assure that the 
work has a positive value in electrolysis mode, where electricity is consumed and the other 
way around in fuel cell mode. The operating voltage of the SOC 𝑉𝑆𝑂𝐶 is calculated with 
 
𝑉SOC = 𝐸Nernst,average −𝐴𝑆𝑅⋅𝑖SOC 
(2.6) 
 
𝐴𝑆𝑅 denotes the area specific resistance of the cell and 𝑖SOC the current density. The averaged 
Nernst potential 𝐸Nernst,average is calculated with 
 
𝐸Nernst,average = Δ𝐺̇
𝐼SOC
 
(2.7) 
==PAGE:40==
2.4   Important components of the system 
16 
 
This is a modification of the definition of the Nernst potential 𝐸Nernst 
 
𝐸Nernst =
Δ𝐺
𝑛𝑒⋅𝐹 
(2.8) 
 
It is achieved by multiplying the numerator and the denominator with the molar flow. Δ𝐺 de-
notes the change in change in Gibbs free energy, 𝑛𝑒 the number of electrons transferred dur-
ing the reaction and 𝐹= 96485 𝐶/𝑚𝑜𝑙 the Faraday constant. 
Δ𝐺̇, used for the calculation of the averaged Nernst potential, is calculated with 
 
Δ𝐺̇ = 𝑚̇ fuel,in 𝑔fuel,in + 𝑚̇ air,in 𝑔air,in −𝑚̇ fuel,out 𝑔fuel,out −𝑚̇ air,out 𝑔air,out 
(2.9) 
 
Where 𝑚̇  denotes the mass flow, 𝑔 the specific Gibbs free energy, the subscripts in and out 
denote the value at the inlet and outlet of a channel respectively and fuel and air denote the 
values in the fuel and air channel respectively. 
Another important parameter for the SOC is the utilization factor. It quantifies, how much of the 
reactants delivered to the SOC stack is converted. For the fuel cell operating mode, the utili-
zation factor 𝑈𝐹 is defined as the ratio of the molar rate of hydrogen consumption over the 
equivalent molar flow of hydrogen supplied to the fuel channel of the SOFC [23]: 
 
𝑈𝐹=
𝑛̇𝐻2,eq,in −𝑛̇𝐻2,eq,out
𝑛̇𝐻2,eq,in
 
(2.10) 
 
𝑛̇𝐻2,eq,in and 𝑛̇𝐻2,eq,out denote the equivalent molar flow rate of hydrogen entering and exiting 
the SOC, respectively. From the reactions, shown in chapter 1.2 it can be found, that the equiv-
alent molar flow rate of hydrogen at the inlet is 
 
𝑛̇𝐻2,eq,in = 𝑛̇𝐻2,in + 𝑛̇𝐶𝑂,in + 4𝑛̇ 𝐶𝐻4,in 
(2.11) 
 
𝑛̇𝑖,𝑖𝑛 denote the molar flow of the species 𝑖 at the inlet of the fuel channel. 𝑛̇𝐻2,eq,out is calcu-
lated analogously, using the molar flow of the species 𝑖 at the outlet of the fuel channel. 
The produced heat during the reactions is proportional to the amount of conversion of the 
reactants. Thus, the utilization factor 𝑈𝐹 can be seen as a factor describing the amount of 
produced heat. The higher 𝑈𝐹, the higher the heat production and the higher the cooling re-
quirements, provided by the air flowing through the oxygen channel [27]. 
Even though it is not that important for the electrolysis mode, a utilization factor 𝑈𝑅 can also 
be defined for the electrolysis operating mode. It is defined as the oxygen generated at the 
oxygen electrode 𝑛̇𝑂2,produced over the equivalent molar flow of oxygen at the inlet of the fuel 
channel 𝑛̇𝑂2,eq,in [23]. 
 
𝑈𝑅=
𝑛̇𝑂2,produced
𝑛̇𝑂2,eq,in
=
𝑛̇𝑂2,eq,in −𝑛̇𝑂2,eq,out
𝑛̇𝑂2,eq,in
 
(2.12)

(2.13) 
 
Another important parameter for the operation of the SOC is the hydrogen to carbon (H/C) 
ratio. For each operating temperature and pressure, a specific minimum H/C ratio is needed 
to avoid formation of solid carbon inside the SOC fuel electrode, which would lead to electrode 
degradation. Figure 2.4 shows the minimum H/C ratio in fuel cell and electrolysis operating 
mode for different temperatures and pressures in the stack[26]. It can be seen that with lower 
pressures and higher temperatures, the minimum H/C ratio in electrolysis mode becomes 
higher than the one in fuel cell mode. 
 
Figure 2.4 Minimum H/C ratio for electrolysis (EL) and fuel cell (FC) operating mode over pressure for different 
operation temperatures of the SOC [26]

3 Results of operation at a pressure of 20 bar 
In this chapter, the results for the operation of the hybrid EES system at a pressure of 20 bar 
are presented. In section 3.1, the design parameters of the system are presented. In section 
3.2 and 3.3, the influence of the amount of syngas from the biomass gasifier used in the system 
is examined for fuel cell and electrolysis operating mode respectively. In section 3.4, the results 
for the entire system are presented, including the energy efficiency definitions helping to ac-
count the energy stream to the different fuels. 
3.1 Design parameters 
In Table 3.1, the process design parameters used in fuel cell and electrolysis operating mode 
are listed. All the values were taken from the paper of Butera et al. [26]. This insures compa-
rability between their system and the new hybrid system.  
The value of ΔTmin, SOC, air channel denotes the minimum temperature differences between the op-
erating temperature of the SOC and the inlet air temperature to the SOC. Additionally, the inlet 
temperature of the air is set to be equal to the inlet temperature of the fuel, if possible. In Table 
3.2, it is shown that within this chapter an operating temperature of the SOC of 𝑇SOC = 700 °C 
is used. This means that the inlet temperature of the air is set to 
 
𝑇𝑎𝑖𝑟,𝑆𝑂𝐶,𝑖𝑛= {𝑇𝑓𝑢𝑒𝑙,𝑆𝑂𝐶,𝑖𝑛    𝑖𝑓 𝑇𝑓𝑢𝑒𝑙,𝑆𝑂𝐶,𝑖𝑛≤650 °𝐶
650 °𝐶           𝑖𝑓 𝑇𝑓𝑢𝑒𝑙,𝑆𝑂𝐶,𝑖𝑛> 650 °𝐶 
(3.1) 
 
The temperature difference for the humidifier and the condensers is set to 5 °C, which is lower 
than the ones for gas-gas (30 °C) and gas-liquid (10 °C) heat exchangers. This is due to the 
direct contact of the gas and the liquid within the humidifier and the condensers. 
The minimum temperature difference for the recuperator, is thus Δ𝑇𝑚𝑖𝑛,𝑟𝑒𝑐𝑢𝑝𝑒𝑟𝑎𝑡𝑜𝑟=
Δ𝑇𝑚𝑖𝑛,𝑔𝑎𝑠−𝑔𝑎𝑠= 30 °𝐶. This means that for fuel inlet temperatures higher than 670 °C, the adi-
abatic methanator is used in fuel cell operating mode to increase the inlet temperature to the 
desired value.

current density and the steam and carbon dioxide content at the outlet of the SOEC were taken

The H/C ratio is set equal for both operating mode to the minimum H/C ratio for the electrolysis 
mode according to Figure 2.4. As the value for the electrolysis mode is higher than the one for 
fuel cell mode, it is insured, that carbon formation is avoided in both operating modes. 
In contrast to the model of Butera where the inlet temperature to the LT-methanator was fixed 
to 220 °C, in this work the outlet temperature of the recuperator in electrolysis mode is fixed to 
243 °C. This temperature derives from the minimum temperature difference of gas-gas heat 
exchangers. However, the outlet temperature of the LT-methanator is set to 280 °C, as done 
by Butera. 
Another important parameter is the oxygen content of the depleted air at the outlet of the air 
channel. This should not be below 5%, in order to avoid problems with the electrochemical 
reactions taking place at the oxygen electrode.

As explained in chapter 2.4.3, the new system design allows for an independent decision for 
the operating pressure of the SOC. Within this chapter, the operating pressure is fixed to 𝑝=
20 bar and the operating temperature to 𝑇𝑆𝑂𝐶= 700 °C. The influence of the variation of the 
syngas mass flow coming from the biomass gasifier is investigated. 
 
3.2 Fuel cell operating mode 
For the simulations of the EES system in fuel cell operating mode, the composition of the 
syngas input coming from the biomass gasifier is set according to Table 2.1. The inlet compo-
sition of the natural gas is set equal to the one of the produced SNG in electrolysis mode. This 
inlet composition was found iteratively. The composition used for an operating pressure of 20 
bar and a temperature of 700 °C is shown in Table 3.3. This composition differs slightly from 
the actual composition of the national pipeline system, as no higher hydrocarbons are present. 
The difference in composition does not have a big impact on the results in fuel cell operating 
mode. However, the use of the same compositions in both operating modes is advantageous, 
as it makes the evaluation of the system easier. The composition of the CO2-rich gas going to 
the storage is a result of the model. 
==PAGE:45==
3   Results of operation at a pressure of 20 bar 
21 
After fixing the compositions and the design parameters, according to Table 3.1 and Table 3.2, 
only one additional parameter can be set as an input to the model. This input has to define the 
ratio between the input mass flows of syngas and NG. This is calculated from the input of the 
fuel inlet temperature to the SOFC 𝑇in,SOFC, the input mass flow of the syngas 𝑚̇ Syn or the input 
mass flow of the natural gas from the gas grid 𝑚̇ NG. One of these values is set as an input, 
while two are outputs of the model. 
Firstly, the influence of the inlet temperature on the amount of syngas used in the system was 
investigated. The first five points in Figure 3.1 depict the syngas mass flows for inlet tempera-
tures to the SOFC of 600 °C, 630 °C, 650 °C, 670 °C and 700 °C. Much higher inlet tempera-
tures than the operation temperature of the SOC are not wanted, as this would induce thermo-
mechanical stress, analogously to lower temperatures, as explained in section 2.4.3. Addition-
ally, too high temperatures could lead to the need of more expensive materials to avoid dam-
ages on the SOC stacks. However, for the sake of completeness, two more points are shown. 
One for the syngas mass flow leading to a NG mass flow of approx. zero at 𝑚̇ Syn = 14.94 kg/s, 
which leads to an inlet temperature to the SOFC of 𝑇in,SOFC = 781.7 °C and one at 𝑚̇ Syn =
7.76 kg/s leading to a mass flow of CO2-rich around zero in electrolysis mode (see section 3.3). 
This leads to an inlet temperature of 𝑇in,SOFC = 709.9 °C 
 
Figure 3.1 Fuel inlet temperature to the SOFC over varying input syngas mass flow 
In Figure 3.1, it can be seen that for inlet temperatures to the SOFC up to 670 °C there is a 
strong increase in the temperature as function of syngas mass flow. For higher temperatures 
the slope is less steep. This is because for higher temperatures, the adiabatic methanator is 
used to achieve the required inlet temperature. This leads to the need of more syngas to reach 
the equilibrium composition at the inlet temperatures to the SOFC above 670 °C. 
In Figure 3.2, the input mass flow of natural gas from the NG grid and the output mass flow of 
CO2-rich gas to the storage are depicted. In this figure, the results of the system of Butera are 
included. They can be found at a syngas mass flow of zero. They are taken from the tables in 
Appendix B. 
It can be seen that both mass flows show a linear behaviour. The mass flow of NG decreases 
with increasing syngas mass flow until reaching approximately zero at a syngas mass flow of 
𝑚̇ Syn = 14.94 kg/s, while the output of CO2-rich gas increases.

==PAGE:47==
3   Results of operation at a pressure of 20 bar 
23 
is equal to the one of the system of Butera, shown for a syngas mass flow of zero. This can be 
explained by looking at Figure 3.4, right. For small syngas mass flows the oxygen content at 
the outlet of the air channel would be lower than 5 %, when using the configuration of Figure 
A.1 on the air side. In order to avoid such low oxygen contents, an air recuperator has to be 
used analogously to Butera’s system, shown in Figure B.1. 
For higher syngas mass flows, the required oxygen content is reached without the use of the 
air recuperator. Higher oxygen contents are beneficial for the stack operation. However, to-
gether with the increasing oxygen content also the air mass flow increases. This leads to bigger 
compressors and turbines on the air side, which will increase the investment costs for the 
system. 
 
Figure 3.4 left: Inlet air mass flow to the system for different syngas mass flows; right: oxygen content at the outlet 
of the oxygen channel for different syngas mass flows 
In Figure 3.5, left, it can be seen that in fuel cell mode the BOP consumes power for all syngas 
mass flows. This has a negative influence on the system performance in fuel cell mode, as the 
purpose of the operating mode is electricity production. However, the power consumption de-
creases with increasing syngas mass flow. 
 
Figure 3.5 left: power consumption of the balance-of-plant for different syngas mass flows; right: power consumption 
of the BOP divided to the different parts of the BOP for different syngas mass flows 
==PAGE:48==
3.2   Fuel cell operating mode 
24 
The power consumption/production of the BOP 𝑊̇ SOFC,BOP consists of four parts (component 
names refer to Figure A.1): 
 
1. Power generation of the storage turbines (TUR1 & TUR2) 
2. Power consumption of the storage compressors (COM1 & COM2) 
3. Power consumption of the air compressor (COM3) 
4. Power generation of the air turbine (TUR3) 
 
The positive trend of the power consumption of the BOP is not provided by the storage turbines 
and compressors. This can be seen by looking at Figure 3.5, right. The power output of the 
storage turbines depends on the NG mass flow entering the system. As the mass flow de-
creases with increasing syngas mass flow (see Figure 3.2), the power output of the storage 
turbines decreases as well. With the same logic, it can be seen that the power consumption of 
the storage compressors increases with increasing mass flow of CO2-rich gas. 
Figure 3.5, right shows that the decreasing power consumption comes from the air side. The 
net power consumption of the air turbine and compressor 𝑊̇ Air,net is calculated with 
 
𝑊̇ Air,net = 𝑚̇ COM ⋅ΔℎCOM −𝑚̇ TUR ⋅ΔℎTUR 
(3.4) 
 
𝑚̇ COM and 𝑚̇ TUR denote the mass flow flowing through the air compressor and turbine respec-
tively and ΔℎCOM and ΔℎTUR denote the enthalpy difference in the compressor and turbine, 
where both are defined in order to have a positive value. 
The compressor increases the pressure of the air, from 𝑝= 1 bar and 𝑇= 20 °C to 𝑝≈20 𝑏𝑎𝑟 
and 𝑇≈470 °𝐶, while the turbine expands between the same pressures from a temperature 
of 𝑇= 700 °𝐶. According to the divergence of isobars with higher temperature it is known, that 
ΔℎTUR > ΔℎCOM. 
Through a mass balance on the air side of the system it can be shown that 
 
𝑚̇ 𝐶𝑂𝑀= 𝑚̇ 𝑇𝑈𝑅+ 𝑚̇ 𝑂2,𝑆𝑂𝐹𝐶 
(3.5) 
 
Where 𝑚̇ 𝑂2,𝑆𝑂𝐹𝐶 denotes the mass flow of oxygen reacting at the oxygen electrode and being 
transferred through the electrode to the fuel electrode. Equation (3.5) can be rearranged to 
 
𝑚̇ TUR
𝑚̇ COM
= 𝑚̇ COM −𝑚̇ O2,SOFC
𝑚̇ COM
= 1 −𝑚̇ O2,SOFC
𝑚̇ COM
 
(3.6) 
 
As within this parameter study of the syngas mass flow, the active area and the current density 
of the SOC remain constant, the total electrical current in the SOFC 𝐼SOFC stays constant. This 
leads also to the mass flow of oxygen reacting at the oxygen electrode remaining constant. 
Thus, equation (3.6) shows that with increasing 𝑚̇ COM, which is equal to the inlet air flow to the 
system, the ratio between 𝑚̇ TUR and 𝑚̇ COM goes towards 1. This and ΔℎTUR > ΔℎCOM lead to-
gether to the decreasing power consumption at the air side of the BOP, which ends in a net 
power production at the air side, as seen in Figure 3.5, right. 
In Figure 3.6, left, the power output of the SOFC 𝑊̇ SOFC is shown for different syngas mass 
flows. It should be noted, that the power output is defined negative, as described in chapter 
1.3. In order to make the figure more intuitive, the absolute value is shown.  
==PAGE:49==
3   Results of operation at a pressure of 20 bar 
25 
The power output of the SOFC does not show a monotone behavior with increasing syngas 
mass flow. As mentioned previously, the total electrical current in the SOC is constant for dif-
ferent syngas mass flows. Thus, the power output of the SOFC depends only on the operating 
voltage 𝑉SOFC, as showed in Equation (2.5). Equations (2.6)-(2.9) show that 𝑉SOFC depends on 
the mass flow and the compositions of the inlet and outlet streams of the SOFC. 
The compositions at the outlet of the SOFC seem to have a strong influence on the perfor-
mance of the SOFC. A strong oxidization of the fuel leads to a lower operating voltage of the 
SOC. In Figure 3.7, left, the molar composition of the CO2-rich gas after the condensers and 
the water removal system is shown. The composition at the outlet of the SOFC is similar. The 
only difference is the presence of steam. It can be seen that the molar ratio of CO2 increases 
while the other molar ratios decrease. This is a sign for strong oxidization, which leads to the 
decreasing power output at the beginning of the diagram until reaching a local minimum at 
𝑚̇ Syn ≈2.5 kg/s. 
As seen in Figure 3.4, right, at this syngas mass flow, the oxygen content at the outlet of the 
air channel starts increasing. The increasing oxygen content has a positive influence on the 
operating voltage. This leads to the increase of the power output, reaching a local maximum 
at 𝑚̇ Syn = 4.5 kg/s. For higher syngas mass flows, the influence of the fuel side increases 
again, leading to a decrease of the power output. 
 
Figure 3.6 left: power output of the SOFC for different syngas mass flows; right net power output of the system in 
fuel cell operating mode for different syngas mass flows 
In Figure 3.6, right, the net power output of the system in fuel cell operating mode is depicted. 
The net power output is calculated with  
 
𝑊̇ SOFC,net = |𝑊̇ SOFC + 𝑊̇ SOFC,BOP| 
(3.7) 
 
As mentioned previously, the power of the SOFC 𝑊̇ SOFC has a negative value, while the power 
consumption of the BOP 𝑊̇ SOFC,BOP is positive. Nevertheless, the absolute value is shown in 
order to make the figure more intuitive. 
It can be seen that at first, when adding syngas to the system, the net power output decreases 
compared to the system of Butera. The reason for this is the worse performance of the SOFC. 
By increasing the syngas mass flow, the power output increases steadily. This shows that the 
power consumption of the BOP decreases faster than the power output of the SOFC. 
==PAGE:50==
3.3   Electrolysis operating mode 
26 
In Figure 3.7,right, the lower heating value (LHV) of the CO2-rich gas is shown for increasing 
syngas mass flows. As seen in Figure 3.7, left, the molar ratios of the high energy gases H2, 
CO and CH4 diminish with increasing syngas mass flow. This leads to the lower heating value 
decreasing as well. The LHV of the CO2-rich gas will be used for the calculations of the energy 
efficiencies in section 3.4.1. 
 
Figure 3.7 left: Molar composition of the CO2-rich gas after the water removal for different syngas mass flows; right: 
Lower heating value of CO2-rich gas after the water removal for different syngas mass flows 
3.3 Electrolysis operating mode 
The results of the calculations in fuel cell operating mode, i.e. the syngas mass flows with the 
corresponding compositions of the CO2-rich gas are used as inputs for the electrolysis operat-
ing mode. As the model is build up analogously to the model of the SOFC mode, this leads to 
have the input mass flow of CO2-rich gas from the storage and the fuel inlet temperature to the 
SOEC as results of the simulations, together with the mass flow and the composition of the 
SNG, exiting the system. 
As mentioned in the beginning of the previous section, the composition of the SNG was found 
iteratively. The calculations showed that the differences in the composition between the differ-
ent simulations are very small. This comes from the constant operation pressure for the differ-
ent simulation and the same operation temperature of the SOEC and outlet temperature of the 
LT-methanator. The resulting composition is shown in Table 3.3.

In Figure 3.10, the fuel inlet temperatures to the SOEC is depicted. It can be seen that it in-

inlet temperature to the SOEC is always higher than the inlet temperature to the SOFC at the 
same syngas mass flow. For a syngas mass flow of 𝑚̇ Syn = 6.84 kg/s, the inlet temperature to 
the SOFC was 𝑇in,SOFC = 700 °C. In electrolysis operating mode the inlet temperature is 
𝑇in,SOEC = 712.9 °C which is higher than the operating temperature of the SOC. As explained in 
the previous section, this is not wanted due to thermal stress and material issues. 
 
Figure 3.10 Fuel inlet temperature to the SOEC over varying input syngas mass flow 
In Figure 3.11, the power consumption of the SOEC 𝑊̇ SOEC, the generated power in the BOP 
𝑊̇ SOEC,BOP and the net power consumption of the system in electrolysis mode 𝑊̇ SOEC,net are 
depicted from left to right. It can be seen that the power consumption of the SOEC and the 
production of the BOP increase slightly with increasing syngas mass flow. 
The jumping increase of 𝑊̇ SOEC does not represent the real development. It derive from the fact 
that the output of DNA is always rounded the fourth digits of the value. This leads also to the 
jumps seen in the development of 𝑊̇ SOEC,net. It is expected that the development of both, 𝑊̇ SOEC 
and 𝑊̇ SOEC,net is monotonous. However, the range in which the values change is very small 
and all in all they remain almost constant.

3.4 Overall results 
As mentioned in section 2.4.6, the mass flow of the syngas is set equal for fuel cell and elec-
trolysis operating mode. Like this, the biomass gasifier will work in full-load during the complete 
operation. 
The two operating modes are additionally connected to each other through the storage tanks 
or cavern used for the storage of the CO2-rich gas. In order to ensure a continuous operation 
of the hybrid EES system, the amount of CO2-rich gas produced during fuel cell operating 
mode 𝑚SOFC and used during electrolysis mode 𝑚SOEC must be equal over a certain period. 
The exact time period depends on the size of the storage, which has to be chosen according 
to the geographical circumstances and to maximize the economy of the system. This is beyond 
the scope of this work and will not be investigated. 
Through setting the mass of produced and used CO2-rich gas in the operating mode equal, 
the capacity factor of each operating mode 𝜏𝑆𝑂𝐸𝐶 and 𝜏𝑆𝑂𝐹𝐶 can be determined as shown in 
Equations (3.10)-(3.13). The capacity factor describes how much time of the design time period 
the system must run in electrolysis and fuel cell operating mode, respectively. 
 
𝑚CO2,SOEC = 𝑚CO2,SOFC 
(3.10) 
𝑚̇ CO2,SOEC ⋅𝜏SOEC = 𝑚̇ CO2,SOFC ⋅𝜏SOFC 
(3.11) 
𝜏SOEC =
𝑚̇ CO2,SOFC
𝑚̇ CO2,SOEC + 𝑚̇ CO2,SOFC
=
𝑡𝑆𝑂𝐸𝐶
𝑡𝑆𝑂𝐸𝐶+ 𝑡𝑆𝑂𝐹𝐶
 
(3.12) 
𝜏SOFC =
𝑚̇ CO2,SOEC
𝑚̇ CO2,SOEC + 𝑚̇ CO2,SOFC
=
𝑡𝑆𝑂𝐹𝐶
𝑡𝑆𝑂𝐸𝐶+ 𝑡𝑆𝑂𝐹𝐶
= 1 −𝜏SOEC 
(3.13) 
 
In these equations, 𝑚̇ CO2,SOEC denotes the input mass flow of CO2-rich gas during electrolysis 
mode and 𝑚̇ CO2,SOFC the output during fuel cell mode. The values for different syngas mass 
flow are depicted in Figure 3.8 and Figure 3.2 respectively. 𝑡SOEC and 𝑡SOFC denote the time in 
which the system works in the respective operating mode. 
In Figure 3.12, the development of the capacity factors 𝜏𝑆𝑂𝐸𝐶 and 𝜏𝑆𝑂𝐹𝐶 are depicted. It can be 
seen that starting from a capacity factor of 50 % in the pure EES system presented by Butera, 
the capacity factor increases/decreases linearly with increasing syngas mass flow until it 
reaches 100 % for electrolysis and 0 % for fuel cell mode at a syngas mass flow of 𝑚̇ Syn =
7.76 kg/s. The increase of the capacity factor 𝜏𝑆𝑂𝐸𝐶 was expected because the outlet mass 
flow of CO2-rich gas in SOFC mode increases, while the input in SOEC mode decreases with 
increasing syngas mass flow. 
Due to the linear correlation between the capacity factor and the syngas mass flow the system 
operation can easily be “tuned” in order to fit the expected capacity factor needed by the elec-
trical grid. In chapter 1 it was mentioned, that the predicted storage needs is around 15-20 % 
of the average annual load [6], [7], which corresponds to 𝜏𝑆𝑂𝐹𝐶= 15 −20 %. Thus, the operat-
ing point with a syngas mass flow of 𝑚̇ Syn = 4.5 kg/s seems to be suitable for this. However, 
further investigations should be done in order to find an optimum operation. This could be done 
by an economic analysis similar to the one conducted by Sigurjonsson and Clausen [28]. 
==PAGE:54==
3.4   Overall results 
30 
 
Figure 3.12 Capacity factor of the fuel cell and electrolysis for different syngas mass flows 
The increasing time in electrolysis mode, together with the higher SNG output in electrolysis 
and lower input in fuel cell mode (see Figure 3.8 and Figure 3.2) will lead to a net SNG pro-
duction of the system in addition to the EES. All the SNG produced during SOEC mode would 
be injected into the natural gas grid and only a part of it will be extracted again during SOFC 
mode. In order to distinguish these parts from each other, the total produced SNG 𝑚SNG,SOEC 
is divided into a stored part 𝑚SNG,stor and a boosted part 𝑚SNG,boost. 
 
𝑚SNG,SOEC = 𝑚SNG,stor + 𝑚SNG,boost 
(3.14) 
 
Where 𝑚SNG,stor is equal to the total used SNG during fuel cell mode 𝑚SNG,SOFC 
 
𝑚SNG,SOFC = 𝑚SNG,stor 
(3.15) 
 
With this distinction, a Sankey-diagram is developed, showing the energy flows in both oper-
ating modes. The energy flows of the material streams 𝐸 are based on the lower heating value 
(LHV) and calculated with 
 
𝐸𝑖,OM = 𝑚̇ 𝑖,OM ⋅𝐿𝐻𝑉𝑖⋅𝜏OM   [kWh/h] 
(3.16) 
 
The subscript 𝑖 denotes the different streams, i.e. SNG, Syn or CO2 and the subscript OM de-
notes the operating mode SOFC of SOEC. The LHV of the CO2-rich stream depends on the 
amount of syngas mass flow and is depicted in Figure 3.7, right. The LHV of the syngas and 
the SNG with the compositions of Table 2.1 and Table 3.3, respectively, are shown in Table 
3.4. 
 
Table 3.4 Lower heating value of syngas and SNG with compositions of Table 2.1 and Table 3.3

In Figure 3.13, two different Sankey diagrams are depicted. The left one is for a syngas mass 
flow of 𝑚̇ Syn = 2.15 kg/s and the right one is for 𝑚̇ Syn = 4.5 kg/s. The energy streams in the 
figures are all normalized to the electricity input in electrolysis operating mode.

It can be seen that with increasing syngas mass flow, the SNG output during SOEC mode

produced in electrolysis mode is used for storage. Therefore, the normalized power output of 
the system corresponds to the roundtrip efficiency of the EES system of 80 %. For a mass flow 
of 𝑚̇ Syn = 7.76 kg/s the system does not store any energy in the NG grid and operates only in 
electrolysis mode and hence does not produce electricity.

3.4.1 Definitions of energy efficiencies 
In order to evaluate the performance of the hybrid EES system, the electrical energy streams 
will be divided in order to be accounted to the different inlet streams, i.e. in electrolysis mode 
to the CO2-rich gas and the syngas and in fuel cell mode to the stored SNG and the syngas. 
This is achieved by introducing different energy efficiencies for both operating modes. 
In the next sections, the definitions of the energy efficiencies for the electrolysis mode, the fuel 
cell mode and the overall are presented together with the results for different syngas mass 
flows. 
3.4.1.1 Electrolysis operating mode 
In electrolysis mode, CO2-rich gas and syngas are converted to SNG using electricity. Thus 
the overall energy efficiency is defined as 
 
𝜂𝑆𝑂𝐸𝐶=
𝐸SNG,SOEC −𝐸CO2,SOEC −𝐸Syn,SOEC
𝑊SOEC,net
 
(3.19) 
 
Where 𝐸i,OM is calculated according to Equation (3.16) and 𝑊SOEC,net according to Equation 
(3.19). 
The total electricity input 𝑊SOEC,net is now divided into two parts. One used to convert the CO2-
rich gas to SNG 𝑊CO2,SOEC and one to convert the syngas 𝑊Syn,SOEC. 
 
𝑊SOEC,net = 𝑊CO2,SOEC + 𝑊Syn,SOEC. 
(3.20) 
 
In order to divide the total amount of produced SNG into the part produced from CO2-rich gas 
and from syngas, the carbon ratios introduced in Equation (3.2) and (3.3) are used. 
The CO2- and syngas-efficiency is now defined as showed in Equation (3.21) and (3.22). These 
definitions are analogous to the overall energy efficiency of the electrolysis operating mode in 
Equation (3.19). The studies of Butera showed that the system efficiency for the conversion of 
CO2-rich gas to SNG at these operating conditions is 89 %, if no syngas is used [26]. Hence, 
the CO2-efficiency in SOEC is set equal to that.  
 
𝜂CO2,SOEC = 𝛾CO2 ⋅𝐸SNG,SOEC −𝐸CO2,SOEC
𝑊𝐶𝑂2,𝑆𝑂𝐸𝐶
= 89 % 
(3.21) 
𝜂Syn,SOEC =
𝛾Syn,SOEC ⋅𝐸SNG,SOEC −𝐸Syn,SOEC
𝑊𝑆𝑦𝑛,𝑆𝑂𝐸𝐶
  
(3.22) 
 
Figure 3.15 shows the development of the different energy efficiencies for the electrolysis op-
erating mode. It can be seen that for a syngas mass flow of zero, the overall efficiency is equal 
to the CO2-efficiency and for 𝑚̇ Syn = 7.76 kg/s it is equal to the syngas efficiency. This can be 
interpreted as a factor of consistency of the definitions because it includes the fact that the 
CO2-rich gas and the syngas are the only gases present at the respective point. The syngas 
efficiency for a syngas mass flow of zero is not shown because it is not possible to define an 
efficiency for that case. 
The fact that the syngas efficiency decreases is surprising, as it would be expected to be con-
stant because syngas and the SNG composition of syngas converted to the same SNG. The 
slight decrease of the efficiency derives from the assumption that the CO2-efficiency is con-
stant. 
==PAGE:57==
3   Results of operation at a pressure of 20 bar 
33 
 
Figure 3.15 Development of the different energy efficiencies for the electrolysis operating mode for different syn-
gas mass flows 
In Figure 3.16, the development of the ratio between 𝑊CO2,SOEC and 𝑊Syn,SOEC is depicted. It 
can be seen that it decreases linearly from 100 % for pure storage use to 0 % where only 
syngas is used. The division of the total energy input in to 𝑊CO2,SOEC and 𝑊Syn,SOEC is based 
on Equation (3.21) and fixing it to 89 %. Using another parameter than the carbon ratio 𝛾CO2 
for the definition would change the division and hence all the results. 
 
Figure 3.16 Development of the ratio between the electricity used for the conversion of CO2-rich gas and syngas 
in electrolysis mode over different syngas mass flows 
3.4.1.2 Fuel cell operating mode 
In fuel cell operating mode, the stored SNG and syngas are used to produce electricity, while 
producing CO2-rich gas. The overall efficiency in fuel cell mode is defined with 
 
𝜂SOFC =
𝑊SOFC,net
𝐸SNG,SOFC + 𝐸Syn,SOFC −𝐸CO2,SOFC
 
(3.23) 
 
The chemical energy of the CO2-rich gas is not considered as a useful product and is thus 
subtracted from the fuel streams 𝐸SNG,SOFC and 𝐸Syn,SOFC. 
As for the electrolysis mode, the electricity output of the SOFC mode is divided into one part 
generated by the conversion of SNG 𝑊SNG,SOFC and one of syngas 𝑊Syn,SOFC. 
 
𝑊SOFC,net = 𝑊SNG,SOFC + 𝑊Syn,SOFC 
(3.24) 
 
==PAGE:58==
3.4   Overall results 
34 
The CO2-rich gas output is divided into a part deriving from SNG and one from syngas by using 
the carbon ratios defined in Equation (3.8) and (3.9). 
The energy efficiency for the electricity generation from SNG and syngas are defined in Equa-
tion (3.25) and (3.26), analogously to Equation (3.23). The efficiency for the electricity produc-
tion with only SNG is 89 % [26]. Hence, the energy efficiency for the electricity generation from 
SNG 𝜂SNG,SOFC is fixed to that value. 
 
𝜂SNG,SOFC =
𝑊SNG,SOFC
𝐸SNG,SOFC −𝛾SNG ⋅𝐸CO2,SOFC
= 89 % 
(3.25) 
𝜂Syn,SOFC =
𝑊Syn,SOFC
𝐸Syn,SOFC −𝛾Syn,SOFC ⋅𝐸CO2,SOFC
 
(3.26) 
 
In Figure 3.17, the energy efficiencies are depicted for different syngas mass flows. As for the 
electrolysis mode, the definitions are consistent because on both limits of the operation, the 
overall efficiency coincides with the SNG- and the syngas efficiency respectively. The overall 
efficiency drops with increasing syngas mass flow, while the syngas efficiency increases. 
 
Figure 3.17 Development of the different energy efficiencies for the fuel cell operating mode for different syngas 
mass flows 
The higher SNG efficiency compared to the syngas efficiency derives from the presence of 
methane, which leads to a more efficient operation of the SOFC. 
Figure 3.18 shows the ratio between the electricity produced from SNG and from syngas. In 
contrast to Figure 3.16, in fuel cell operating mode the ratio is not linear.  
 
Figure 3.18 Development of the ratio between the electricity generation from the conversion of SNG and syngas in 
fuel cell operating mode over different syngas mass flows

3   Results of operation at a pressure of 20 bar

3.4.1.3 Overall system 
With the help of the division of the electricity streams and the product gas streams of each 
operating mode, the hybrid EES system can be divided into three subsystems. One electricity 
storage system, one syngas upgrading system and one electricity producing and syngas up-
grading system. In Figure 3.19, a detailed Sankey diagram of the system with a syngas mass 
flow of 𝑚̇ Syn = 4.5 kg/s is depicted. In the figure, the different subsystems can be seen. 
The electricity storage system works as the usual EES systems based on SOCs. In fuel cell 
mode the stored SNG is converted to electricity and the CO2-rich gas is stored. This CO2-rich 
gas is then reconverted in electrolysis mode using electricity from the grid. 
 
Figure 3.19 Detailed Sankey diagram showing all electrical and chemical energy flows (LHV) and conversion heat 
losses and the division into three subsystems for hybrid EES system working with a syngas input of 4.5 kg/s. Note: 
All flows are normalized to the net electricity input to the SOEC  
For the electricity storage subsystem a roundtrip efficiency is defined with 
 
𝜂RT = 𝑊SNG,SOFC
𝑊CO2,stor
= 17%
21% = 79% 
(3.27) 
 
Hereby 𝑊CO2,stor denotes the electricity used to convert CO2-rich gas with the purpose of stor-
ing energy. Example values refer to the streams of the electricity storage subsystem in Figure 
3.19. 
In Figure 3.19, it can be seen that not all the CO2-rich gas is used in the electricity storage 
subsystem. Therefore, the electricity used to convert CO2-rich gas to SNG 𝑊CO2,SOEC is divided 
into one used for storing electricity 𝑊CO2,stor and one part upgrading the CO2-rich gas to SNG 
𝑊CO2,boost. For the division of the electricity consumption, the inlet carbon ratio of the fuel cell 
operating mode is used, as it was done for dividing the chemical energy of the CO2-rich gas in 
Equation (3.25) and (3.26).
