𝑊CO2,SOEC = 𝑊CO2,stor + 𝑊CO2,up = 𝛾Syn,SOFC ⋅𝑊CO2,SOEC + 𝛾SNG ⋅𝑊CO2,SOEC 
(3.28) 
 
In Figure 3.20, the roundtrip efficiency is shown for different syngas mass flows. The dashed 
line and the point at a syngas mass flow of zero denote a roundtrip efficiency of 𝜂RT = 79.2 %, 
which is the power of two of 89 %. Due to rounding errors, this value does not respect the 80% 
roundtrip efficiency calculated by Butera [26].  
It can be seen that the storage roundtrip efficiency of the hybrid EES system achieves approx-
imately the same value, which indicates that the definitions of the energy efficiency and the 
division of the energy stream are reasonable and consistent. 
 
Figure 3.20 Storage roundtrip efficiency of the hybrid EES system for different syngas mass flows. The dashed 
lines denotes a round trip efficiency of 79.2 %, accounting for an efficiency of 89 % for both electrolysis and fuel 
cell operating mode 
The small deviations from the calculated value of 𝜂RT = 79.2 % are retained to derive from 
rounding errors during the extraction of the results from DNA and from small differences of the 
gas compositions between the model for the electrolysis and the fuel cell operating mode. 
The second subsystem is the syngas upgrading system, in which the syngas entering the sys-
tem in electrolysis mode is directly converted to SNG. The efficiency of this subsystem is the 
syngas efficiency of the electrolysis mode, defined in Equation (3.22) and depicted in Figure 
3.15. 
 
𝜂Syn,SOEC =
𝛾Syn,SOEC ⋅𝐸SNG,SOEC −𝐸Syn,SOEC
𝑊𝑆𝑦𝑛,𝑆𝑂𝐸𝐶
= 95 % −50%
57 %
= 79% 
(3.22) 
 
The example values refer to the streams of the electricity storage subsystem in Figure 3.19. 
The third subsystem is the electricity production and syngas upgrading system. In this system, 
syngas is first used to produce electricity in fuel cell operating mode and the produced CO2-
rich gas is then used in electrolysis mode to produce SNG. For this system the energy effi-
ciency is defined as 
 
𝜂Syn,SOFC =
(𝛾CO2 ⋅𝐸SNG,SOEC −𝐸SNG,SOFC) −𝐸Syn,SOFC + 𝑊Syn,SOFC
𝑊CO2,Up
= 25% −13% + 4%
22%
= 74% 
(3.29) 
 
==PAGE:61==
3   Results of operation at a pressure of 20 bar 
37 
The first two terms in the numerator (𝛾CO2 ⋅𝐸SNG,SOEC −𝐸SNG,SOFC) denote the part of SNG pro-
duced in this subsystem. It is the part of SNG produced from CO2-rich gas (𝛾CO2 ⋅𝐸SNG,SOEC) 
and not used to store energy in the NG grid (−𝐸SNG,SOFC). The chemical energy of the added 
syngas in SOFC mode is subtracted from the product SNG, as it was done for the other effi-
ciencies including SNG production. In addition, the electricity production during the fuel cell 
operating mode is regarded as a product and not subtracted from the consumed electricity in 
electrolysis mode. This is done because the production and the consumption are not at the 
same time. The example values refer to the streams of the electricity storage subsystem in 
Figure 3.19. 
In Figure 3.21, the development of the energetic efficiency for the three subsystems is de-
picted. For a syngas mass flow of zero and of 𝑚̇ Syn = 7.76 kg/s, not all points are shown be-
cause both cases the subsystem is not present due to pure electricity storage operation and 
no operation in fuel cell mode respectively. 
 
 
Figure 3.21 Development of the energetic efficiency of the different subsystems over syngas mass flow 
It can be seen that the efficiency increases with increasing syngas mass flow. This comes from 
the increasing syngas efficiency in fuel cell operating mode, seen in Figure 3.17 and the, by 
definition, constant CO2-efficiency in electrolysis mode. 
 
In order to evaluate the upgrading performance of syngas to gas grid quality SNG, both sub-
systems producing SNG are compared to conventional bio-syngas upgrading. Under conven-
tional bio-syngas upgrading, the state-of-the-art system is intended, using an SOEC in parallel 
to a methanation reactor. Hence, for conventional bio-syngas upgrading, the syngas is not 
used within the electrolysis cell. The electrolysis cell is used to produce the required amount 
of hydrogen through water electrolysis. From the reaction equations (2.1) and (2.2) the required 
amount of hydrogen 𝑛̇𝐻2,need can be extracted as: 
 
𝑛̇𝐻2,need = 4 ⋅𝑛̇𝐶𝑂2,Syn,SOEC + 3 ⋅𝑛̇𝐶𝑂,Syn,SOEC −𝑛̇𝐻2,Syn,SOEC 
(3.30) 
 
Where 𝑛̇𝑖,Syn,SOEC denotes the molar flow rate of the species 𝑖 entering the system with the 
syngas from the biomass gasifier during electrolysis operating mode. Clausen examined a 
==PAGE:62==
3.4   Overall results 
38 
conventional syngas upgrading system using a SOEC and showed that the hydrogen electro-
lyser can achieve an efficiency of 𝜂SOEC,convUp = 92 % based on the chemical energy of the 
hydrogen [34]. 
 
𝜂SOEC,convUp =
𝐸̇𝐻2,need
𝑊̇ SOEC,convUp
= 𝑛̇𝐻2,need ⋅𝐿𝐻𝑉
̅̅̅̅̅̅𝐻2
𝑊̇ SOEC,convUp
 
(3.31) 
 
𝑊̇ SOEC,convUp denotes the power consumption of the SOEC stack during steam electrolysis and 
𝐿𝐻𝑉
̅̅̅̅̅̅𝐻2 denotes the molar LHV of hydrogen.  
In order to evaluate upgrading systems, usually an output to input efficiency is used. For the 
conventional upgrading the upgrading efficiency 𝜂convUp is defined as 
 
𝜂convUp =
𝐸̇𝑆𝑁𝐺
𝑊̇ SOEC,convUp + 𝐸̇Syn
=
𝛾Syn,SOEC ⋅𝐸̇SNG,SOEC
𝑊̇ SOEC,convUp + 𝐸̇Syn,SOEC
 
(3.32) 
 
In this work, 𝜂convUp is calculated by using the chemical energy input from syngas in electrol-
ysis mode 𝐸̇Syn,SOEC and the SNG produced from the syngas 𝛾Syn,SOEC ⋅𝐸̇SNG,SOEC. 
In the same way, the upgrading efficiency of the syngas upgrading subsystem 𝜂Up,SOEC is de-
fined. The only difference is the power consumption 𝑊̇ Syn,SOEC. 
 
𝜂Up,SOEC =
𝛾Syn,SOEC ⋅𝐸̇SNG,SOEC
𝑊̇ Syn,SOEC + 𝐸̇Syn,SOEC
=
𝛾Syn,SOEC ⋅𝐸SNG,SOEC
𝑊Syn,SOEC + 𝐸Syn,SOEC
=
95%
57% + 50% = 89% 
(3.33) 
 
It can be seen that this can be done using both power and energy data. This is because the 
syngas upgrading subsystem works only in electrolysis mode. In contrast to this, the upgrading 
efficiency of the electricity production and syngas upgrading subsystem 𝜂Up,SOFC must be de-
fined by using energies because it works in both operating modes. 
 
𝜂Up,SOFC =
(1 −εSNG) ⋅𝛾CO2 ⋅𝐸SNG,SOEC
𝑊CO2,Up −𝑊Syn,SOFC + 𝐸Syn,SOFC
=
25%
22% −4% + 13% = 82% 
(3.34) 
 
By subtracting the electricity output in fuel cell operating mode 𝑊Syn,SOFC from the consumption 
in electrolysis mode 𝑊CO2,boost, a net power consumption is used in this definition. This was 
avoided in equation (3.29), because of the different moments in which they are produced/con-
sumed. However, in the definition of a syngas upgrading efficiency, electricity is not a wanted 
product, but it is still beneficial for the system performance. Therefore, it is included in the 
denominator. In equation (3.33) and (3.34), the given percentages refer to Figure 3.19. 
In Figure 3.22, the upgrading efficiency for the conventional upgrading system and the hybrid 
EES system are depicted. For the efficiency of the conventional upgrading, the power con-
sumption was calculated for the syngas composition showed in Table 2.1. The output compo-
sition of the SNG of the conventional upgrading system was assumed to be equal to the one 
of the hybrid EES system. 
It can be seen that the upgrade efficiency of both subsystems and the total hybrid EES system 
are higher than the one of the conventional upgrading. This comes from the better performance 
of the SOEC when hydrocarbons are present within the cell. 
==PAGE:63==
3   Results of operation at a pressure of 20 bar 
39 
The higher efficiency of the syngas upgrading subsystem compared to the electricity produc-
tion and syngas upgrading makes also sense, because the syngas is first oxidized in SOFC 
mode and then reduced to SNG in SOEC mode. It can also be seen that the overall efficiency 
of the hybrid EES system approaches the efficiency of the syngas upgrading system with in-
creasing syngas mass flows until reaching it for 𝑚̇ Syn = 7.76 kg/s. The reason for this is that 
with increasing syngas mass flow more of the SNG produced in the system is produced in the 
syngas upgrading and less in the electricity production and syngas upgrading subsystem. For 
𝑚̇ Syn = 7.76 kg/s no SNG is produced through the latter one. Therefore, in Figure 3.22, no 
value for the efficiency 𝜂Up,SOFC is given at 𝑚̇ Syn = 7.76 kg/s. 
 
 
Figure 3.22 Upgrading efficiency for the conventional upgrading and the hybrid EES system and its SNG producing 
subsystems for different syngas mass flows 
Another way of showing the different performances of the syngas upgrading systems is by 
comparing the power consumption for the upgrading of a certain amount of syngas. The power 
consumption can be calculated for a certain mass or chemical energy of syngas. In Figure 
3.23, the results for this are shown. The calculated values are normalized to the power con-
sumption of the conventional upgrading system. As expected from the higher efficiency of the 
upgrading in the hybrid EES system, the electricity consumption is lower than for the conven-
tional upgrading. 
 
Figure 3.23 Electricity consumption for conventional upgrading and the hybrid EES system and its SNG producing 
subsystems for different syngas mass flows. Note: the consumptions are normalized to the power consumption of 
the conventional upgrading system 
==PAGE:64==
3.5   Alternative operation of the system 
40 
To conclude this chapter, an overall energy efficiency of the hybrid EES system is defined and 
evaluated. It is defined as 
 
𝜂𝑡𝑜𝑡=
𝑊SOFC + (𝐸SNG,SOEC −𝐸SNG,SOFC) −𝐸Syn,SOEC −𝐸Syn,SOFC
𝑊𝑆𝑂𝐸𝐶
 
(3.35) 
 
(𝐸SNG,SOEC −𝐸SNG,SOFC) describes the net output of chemical energy of the produced SNG. In 
Figure 3.24, the development of the overall energy efficiency is depicted for different syngas 
mass flows. It can be seen that for a syngas mass flow of zero, it corresponds to the roundtrip 
efficiency of the pure EES system and for 𝑚̇ Syn = 7.76 kg/s it corresponds to the efficiency of 
the electrolysis operating mode (see Figure 3.15). This makes sense because for this mass 
flow, the system operates only in electrolysis mode. For this reason, this definition of the overall 
energy efficiency was preferred to other possibilities. 
 
Figure 3.24 Overall energy efficiency of the hybrid EES system for different syngas mass flows 
3.4.2 Discussion of results for the overall system 
The results for the overall system shown in this section are based on the definition of the 
different energy efficiencies. These definitions are based on dividing energy streams according 
to the carbon ratio of the input gas streams to the system. Other definitions have been tried 
during the work on this thesis, using e.g. ratios between the chemical energy of the input 
streams or connecting the factor 𝛾 for the division of the energy streams to design parameters 
as the utilization factor. None of these definitions achieved the consistency of the definitions 
used here. The consistency of the definitions is seen in the fact that at the limits of the systems, 
i.e. syngas mass flow of zero and CO2-rich gas mass flow of zero, all the energy efficiencies 
for the overall system coincide with the values of a pure EES system or a pure syngas upgrad-
ing system respectively. 
3.5 Alternative operation of the system 
Within this work, the biomass gasifier is not examined in detail. However, it was mentioned in 
chapter 2.4.6 that the biomass gasifier needs a certain amount of heat to dry the biomass. If 
the heat output of the system is not sufficient when working as presented in this chapter, 
changes in the operation of the system might be necessary. 
A higher heat output is especially interesting for a new design of the two-stage biomass gasi-
fier, presented by Clausen [34]. The new design uses an updraft fixed bed reactor instead of 
a screw conveyer for the pyrolysis and a second updraft fixed bed reactor instead of a 
downdraft fixed bed reactor for the char gasification. This brings several advantages compared 
==PAGE:65==
3   Results of operation at a pressure of 20 bar 
41 
to the design presented in chapter 2.4.6. However, its heat demand is higher and thus it could 
be necessary to increase the heat output from the hybrid EES system by changing the opera-
tion of the adiabatic methanator, particularly in fuel cell operating mode. Instead of using it only 
for input temperatures to the SOFC of 𝑇in,SOFC > 670 °𝐶, this reactor could also be used for 
lower inlet temperatures. Calculations for the use of the adiabatic methanator to increase the 
temperature from an outlet temperature of the recuperator 𝑇out,Recuperator = 600 °𝐶 to the re-
quired inlet temperature to the SOFC were conducted. In Figure 3.25, it can be seen that the 
energetic efficiency in fuel cell operation mode, defined with Equation (3.23), is slightly lower 
when using the adiabatic methanator for 𝑇in,SOFC > 600 °𝐶 instead of 𝑇in,SOFC > 670 °𝐶. 
 
Figure 3.25 Energetic efficiency of the hybrid EES system in fuel cell operating mode for different uses of the 
adiabatic methanator 
On the other hand, using the adiabatic methanator for lower temperatures increases the heat 
output of the system. In Figure 3.26, the heat output of HEX5 (see Appendix A) is depicted for 
both operations of the adiabatic methanator. It can be seen that the heat output for the alter-
native use of the adiabatic methanator is higher. The reason for this is that the required heat 
transfer within the recuperator is decreased by using the adiabatic methanator at lower tem-
peratures. This leads to higher temperatures of the gas stream going to HEX 5 (stream 12 in 
Appendix A). Additionally, this is beneficial for the size of the recuperator, because the heat 
transfer is decreased while the temperature difference between the gases is increased. This 
topic is further discussed in chapter 7 of Part II of this work. 
==PAGE:66==
3.5   Alternative operation of the system 
42 
 
Figure 3.26 Heat output of HEX5 in fuel cell operating mode for different uses of the adiabatic methanator 
The new design of a hybrid EES system introduces also another possibility for alternative op-
eration. The system can operate very flexibly by operating in part-load in both operating modes. 
This can be achieved by using only syngas and no NG in fuel cell operating mode and no CO2-
rich gas in electrolysis operating mode. In this way, part-load operation of the hybrid EES sys-
tem is achieved, while having full-load operation of the two-stage gasifier. 
In Figure 3.1 and Figure 3.10 it was shown that with pure syngas as an input (highest syngas 
input to system) the input temperatures to the SOC are higher. This can cause problems due 
to thermo-mechanical stress or material damaging. Clausen examined a bio-syngas upgrading 
system using pressurized SOEC and pure bio-syngas as an input [34]. There the high inlet 
temperature was avoided by using two adiabatic methanators with an intercooling between 
them. The excess heat can be used for the drying in the two-stage gasifier. 
Even though the use of two adiabatic methanators increases the investment cost of the sys-
tem, it introduces the ability of a higher adaptability of the hybrid EES system to fluctuating 
electricity prices. In Figure 3.27, the idea of how to operate the system according to the elec-
tricity price is depicted.  
 
Figure 3.27 Operation of the hybrid EES system for different electricity prices 
==PAGE:67==
3   Results of operation at a pressure of 20 bar 
43 
At very low electricity prices, the system operates in full-load electrolysis mode, using the max-
imum electricity to upgrade syngas and CO2-rich gas to SNG. In this operating mode, the sys-
tem works as explained in chapter 2.3.  
At moderately low electricity prices, the system operates in part-load electrolysis mode, up-
grading only syngas to SNG. 
At moderately high electricity prices, the system operates in part-load fuel cell mode, oxidizing 
only syngas. The CO2-rich gas would then be released to the environment as exhaust gas, as 
its composition is different from the one produced in full-load fuel cell mode: the storage of this 
CO2-rich stream would affect the following electrolysis mode as the inlet stream would vary 
from the usual composition of the CO2-storage. As the CO2-rich gas is not used for later up-
grade to SNG in electrolysis operating mode, an additional gas burner-turbine system could 
be used instead, thus increasing the overall electricity output of the system.  
At high electricity prices, the system operates in full-load fuel cell mode oxidizing syngas and 
NG from the grid. The CO2-rich gas is then stored in the storage for later upgrading in full-load 
electrolysis mode. 
At very high electricity prices, the electricity production could be boosted by using the above 
mentioned gas burner-turbine system. The exhaust gas of the system would then be released 
to the environment and not stored in the storage of the CO2-rich gas. Note that the release of 
the CO2-rich gas in the atmosphere corresponds to a carbon-neutral stream, since the carbon 
contained in this flow is re-introduced in the system via biomass. 
==PAGE:68==
 
 
 
==PAGE:69==
4   Conclusion 
45 
4 Conclusion 
Within this study, a hybrid EES system, based on pressurized ReSOCs combined with a two-
stage gasifier was proposed. The injection of syngas from the biomass gasifier enables the 
system to store energy in the natural gas grid and to produce additional SNG from the syngas. 
Through the addition of syngas, the catalytic recuperator, needed in fuel cell operating mode 
of the system of Butera et al. [26] can be avoided. This leads to an operating pressure, which 
is independent of the inlet temperature to the SOFC. 
For an operating pressure of the SOC of 𝑝= 20 bar  and an operating temperature of 
𝑇=  700 °C, the influence of different syngas mass flows was examined. The inlet temperature 
to the SOC in both operating modes increases with increasing syngas mass flows. This allows 
to achieve SOC inlet temperatures near to the operating temperature, which is beneficial for 
the operation of the SOC. 
It was found that by varying the mass flow of syngas used in the hybrid EES system, the 
capacity factor of each operating mode can be changed. This introduces the possibility to 
“tune” the operation according to the requirements of the electrical grid or in order to maximize 
the revenue of the systems. Usually, it is assumed that the electricity price behaves similar to 
the requirements of the electrical grid, i.e. high prices in case of high demand and low prices 
for low demand. Assuming this, an operation with a syngas mass flow of  𝑚̇ Syn =  4.5 kg/s 
would be the most interesting of the simulated cases, because with a capacity factor in fuel 
cell operating mode of approximately 20 % it fits to the expected electricity storage demand of 
15-20 % predicted by [6], [7]. Additionally, the inlet temperature to the SOFC of 
𝑇in,SOFC =  670 °𝐶 and to the SOEC of 𝑇in,SOEC =  681 °𝐶 ensures low thermo-mechanical 
stresses on the SOC 
In order to evaluate the system performance, the conversion efficiency was calculated sepa-
rately for each input stream of each operation mode. This was done by accounting different 
parts of the outlet gas streams, i.e. SNG in electrolysis and CO2-rich gas in fuel cell mode, to 
the inlet gas streams of the respective operating mode, according to the carbon content of the 
respective inlet gas. Electricity streams were split using conversion efficiencies of 89 % for the 
conversion of electricity and CO2-rich gas to SNG in electrolysis operating mode and SNG to 
electricity and CO2-rich gas, according to the results of Butera et al. 
This led to the division of the system into three different subsystems, an energy storing sub-
system, a syngas upgrading subsystem and an energy producing and syngas upgrading sub-
system. 
The upgrading efficiency of the syngas upgrading subsystem was found to be always higher 
than 88 % and for the energy producing and syngas upgrading subsystem always higher than 
81.5 %. The overall upgrading efficiency of the hybrid EES is between 86.9 % and 88.1 % for 
different input syngas mass flows. 
These were compared to conventional bio-syngas upgrading using an SOEC to produce hy-
drogen in parallel to a methanation reactor. It showed that the upgrading efficiency is higher 
for the hybrid EES system than for the conventional bio-syngas upgrading with a maximum 
upgrading efficiency of 78 %.  
Finally, alternative operation ways of the system were presented, allowing for a higher heat 
output of the system or for a more flexible operation following the electricity price. 
Summarizing, the results show that the hybrid EES system introduces the possibility of pro-
ducing SNG and storing electricity efficiently and introduces the possibility to tune the operation 
in order to operate non-stop and to maximize the revenue of the system. 
 
==PAGE:70==
4.1   Outlook 
46 
4.1 Outlook 
Further investigations of the hybrid EES system should be done. Especially variations of the 
operating pressure and temperature of the SOC should be conducted in order to find optimal 
operating conditions. 
Afterwards, the economic feasibility of the system based on variable electricity prices should 
be assessed. This could include part-load operation in both operation modes, as explained in 
section 3.5. Further evaluation of the part-load operation has to be done because it could re-
quire changes in the system configuration, including a second adiabatic methanator. The eco-
nomic analysis would include an optimization model to find the optimal operation between the 
different operation modes 
For the economic analysis, the dimensions of the two-stage biomass gasifier will be required 
in order to estimate the investment costs. Hence, a suitable two-stage gasifier has to be di-
mensioned according to the systems requirements. This is also necessary in order to ensure 
that the heat output of the system is sufficient for the drying of the biomass feed.

==PAGE:93==
 
 
 
 
 
 
Part II 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Design of an integrated SOC stack in 
a heat exchanger 
 
 
==PAGE:94==
 
 
 
==PAGE:95==
Abstract 
i 
 
Abstract 
In Part I of this work it was shown that solid oxide cells can be efficiently used to store electricity 
as synthetic natural gas (SNG) or to upgrade bio-syngas to SNG. This technology is based on 
pressurized operation of solid oxide cells. The high pressures combined with the high operation 
temperatures around 700 °C lead to high stresses for the pressure bearing structure around 
the solid oxide cell stack. 
Additionally, the technology presented in Part 1 requires the use of a gas recuperator between 
the inlet and outlet streams of the solid oxide cell. For small-scale applications, this heat ex-
changer is one of the most expensive components of the system. 
Within this part of the work, a new design of an integrated solid oxide cell stack in a heat 
exchanger is presented. This design combines the pressure vessel with the recuperator, by 
having a cylindrical heat exchanger inside of the pressure bearing steel. By having the hot end 
of the heat exchanger near the solid oxide stack and the cold end on the outside, the pressure 
bearing steel is in contact with cold gas. This reduces the thermal stress of the pressure bear-
ing shell. 
For an evaluation of the new design, a detailed model for the calculation of a cylindrical heat 
exchanger was built in the software EES. The model includes correlations for the convective 
heat transfer coefficient and the pressure loss in annular gaps as well as for the calculation of 
fluid properties for gas mixtures. Additionally, the mechanical strength of the cylindrical heat 
transfer shells was included, in order to insure a long-term operation of the heat exchanger. 
With this model, the design of the integrated SOC stack in a HEX was examined for two differ-
ent systems. The first system requires a catalytic reaction within the HEX in fuel cell operating 
mode, while in the second system this is not required.  
For both systems, the new design was compared to a reference case consisting of a commer-
cial pressure vessel and a plate heat exchanger. Water and gas cooling of the pressure bear-
ing shell were assessed. The heat losses were calculated using EES. The results showed that 
the water-cooled pressure vessel performs better than the gas-cooled one. The dimensions of 
the plate heat exchanger were calculated using the SSP G7 software of the company SWEP 
©. 
The comparison between the systems was done by comparing the total mass of steel, which 
is an indication for the material cost of the systems. For the system with a catalytic reaction 
the new design requires at least 3.8 times the amount of steel of the reference case using a 
water-cooled pressure vessel. In the system without the catalytic reaction, the factor is reduced 
to a minimum of 2.4. 
Finally, an improved design of the integrated solid oxide cell stack in a heat exchanger is pre-
sented, including water cooling of the pressure bearing steel. With this improvement, the mass 
ratio can be reduced to a minimum of 1.8.

==PAGE:111==
1   Introduction 
1 
1 Introduction 
In Part I of this work, an electrical energy storage system using pressurized solid oxide cells 
(SOC) was presented. The use of pressurized SOCs has many advantages for the cost and 
efficiency of the overall system. However, the pressurized operation of the SOC leads to the 
necessity of a pressure vessel in order to insure matching pressure within the SOC stack and 
the surrounding gas.  
One potential problem is the mechanical strength of the pressure-bearing steel surrounding 
the SOC stack. The steel mechanical strength is strongly related to the temperature as can be 
seen in Figure 1.1. This, combined with the operation at elevated pressures, leads to the ne-
cessity of cooling the outer surface of the pressure vessel to insure the mechanical stability, 
thus minimizing the amount of steel and the weight of the pressure-bearing shell surrounding 
the stack (see also Appendix B). Usually the cooling of the outer steel shell is conducted 
through a secondary fluid, flowing on the outside (See Figure B.1). 
 
Figure 1.1 Temperature dependency of yield strength for stainless steel 304/304L (interpolated from data from [1]) 
An alternative way to assure a cooling of the pressure-bearing steel is to integrate a heat ex-
changer between the stack on the inside and the pressure-bearing shell on the outside. As the 
cold fluid entering the fuel electrode compartment, i.e. the anode compartment in fuel cell op-
eration mode and the cathode compartment in electrolysis operation mode (See explanation 
in Part I), has to be heated up by the hot fluid exiting the same compartment, this heat ex-
changer is especially suited for being integrated with the stack. In the figures in 0 the corre-
sponding heat exchanger for the SOEC and SOFC operating mode (referred to as Catalytic 
Heat recuperator) of the electrical storage system investigated by Butera [2],[3] is highlighted 
with a green rectangle. 
Especially for smaller de-centralized plants, this seems to be an interesting solution, as the 
heat exchanger is one of the most expensive balance-of-plant components for these plants [4], 
[5].  
In the following chapters the principal design idea for the heat exchanger and the model used 
to evaluate it are explained. Using the model, the technical feasibility of such an integrated 
SOC stack in a heat exchanger is investigated. Therefore, calculations are conducted, design-
ing different stack sizes for electrical storage systems using pressurized reversible solid oxide 
cells without biomass gasification, which have been investigated by G. Butera [3]. The results 
==PAGE:112==
1   Introduction 
2 
 
for the integrated SOC stack with heat exchanger are then compared to a reference case in 
which a standard pressure vessel and external plate heat exchangers are used. 
In chapter 2 the principle design idea of the integrated SOC stack in a heat exchanger is ex-
plained In chapter 3, the mathematical model of the heat exchanger, used to calculate the 
performance and the size of it, and in chapter 4 its implementation in the software “Engineering 
Equation Solver” (EES) are described. The new design of the integrated SOC stack is com-
pared to a reference case, using a standard pressure vessel and an external plate heat ex-
changer. This is explained in chapter 5. In chapter 6, the models are applied for the system 
proposed by Butera and the results of the integrated SOC stack and the reference case are 
compared. In chapter 7, the calculations are repeated for the new hybrid electrical energy 
storing system, introduced in Part I of this work. Finally, in section 8, the results are summa-
rized and an outlook on further solutions for the integration of an SOC stacks in a heat ex-
changer is analysed. 
==PAGE:113==
2   Design of an integrated SOC stack in a heat exchanger 
3 
2 Design of an integrated SOC stack in a heat exchanger 
The principal design idea for the integrated SOC stack in a heat exchanger is simple. The 
planar SOC stack is placed inside of several circular tubes in which the heat transfer between 
the entering and exiting fuel occurs. In Figure 2.1, the basic idea of an integrated SOC stack 
in a heat exchanger is depicted. 
 
Figure 2.1 SOC stack integrated in a heat exchanger [6] 
Depending on the quantity of heat required to be transferred to meet the requirements of the 
system, it is possible that more than one heat exchange layer is needed. One heat exchange 
layer is defined as two annular chambers in which the hot and the cold fluid flow, plus the 
necessary steel tubes and one insulation layer. The first heat exchange layer is depicted in 
Figure 2.2. Here the grey areas stand for steel walls and the yellow one depicts the insulation. 
In the spaces between the steel shells the hot and cold fluid flow. A second layer is then added 
on the outside of layer one, using the same configuration. 
 
Figure 2.2 Top view on the first heat exchanger layer with dimensions of the SOC stack and the inner diameter. 
As for any other heat exchanger, it is important to define a flow configuration. Here, a counter 
flow geometry between the hot and the cold fluids was considered. This assures the most 
==PAGE:114==
2   Design of an integrated SOC stack in a heat exchanger 
4 
 
efficient heat transfer and therefore minimizes the necessary heat transfer area, i.e. the num-
ber of heat exchange layers. 
One of the main goals of the design of an integrated SOC stack in heat exchanger is to reduce 
the investment cost compared to the cost of an external heat exchanger. In order to achieve 
this, the design should be as simple as possible to assure that the manufacturing costs are 
low. For the flow configuration, this is achieved by alternating the location of the hot and the 
cold fluid in every layer. This means, if in the first layer the hot fluid is on the inside and the 
cold fluid on the outside, in the second the cold one is inside and the hot one on the outside. 
Therefore, the configuration is HC-CH-HC-…, with H denominating the hot fluid and C the cold 
fluid. From a manufacturing point of view, this is a very simple configuration because it is pos-
sible to have the cold and hot fluid flowing parallel to each other through the whole heat ex-
changer. The configuration is shown in Figure 2.3, left. The heat exchanger is built vertically, 
whereas the horizontal representation used in Figure 2.3 insures to keep it easily readable. 
The alternative solution would be to use a HC-HC-HC… configuration. This configuration has 
the advantage to assure that the cold fluid will always be on the outside of the last layer, no 
matter how many layers are necessary to transfer the required heat. The big disadvantage of 
this configuration is the need of crossing the hot and cold fluid after every layer. This would 
increase the manufacturing effort for the integrated SOC stack and therefore the investment 
costs. An additional pressure loss would be induced because the cross-sectional area has to 
be halved for the hot and the cold fluid in order to enable the crossing between the fluids. (See 
Figure 2.3, right) 
In Figure 2.2 and Figure 2.3 there is depicted an insulation between every heat exchange 
layer. Part of this work was to examine the influence of the thickness of this insulation 
𝛿𝐼𝑛𝑠𝑢𝑙𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛. The influence was examined by conducting a parameter variation, as described in 
chapter 6.1.4. 
For the walls of the circular tube heat exchanger, type 304 stainless steel is used as material. 
It is a commonly used material, able to support the temperatures of more than 1000 °C [1].The 
selection of the most suitable material is beyond the scope of this work. 
 
 
Figure 2.3 left: HC-CH-HC flow configuration; right: HC-HC-HC flow configuration (red denotes the hot fluid and 
blue the cold fluid) 
Previous investigations of S. H. Jensen showed that the pressure loss within the SOC stack 
decreases with increasing operating pressure. Thus, high pressure SOCs allow for big surface 
stacks. [7]. For this reason, the measures of the side length of the quadratic stack is chosen

to 𝐿𝑐𝑒𝑙𝑙= 50 cm, leading to a diagonal of 70.7 cm. To assure sufficient space for ducts, meas-
urement equipment, insulations etc. an inner diameter for the circular tube heat exchanger of 
𝐷0 = 80 cm is applied.

denotes the active area of one SOC cell, derived from the side length and 𝑎Stack denotes the 
active area per meter stack height.

Within this work, only the part of the system where the heat transfer occurs is considered. I.e. 
the top and the bottom of the pressure bearing structure as well as the ducts and piping con-
necting the heat exchanger to the other components of the system are not considered.

3   Mathematical model of the new heat exchanger design 
7 
3 Mathematical model of the new heat exchanger design 
In this chapter, the model for the evaluation of the integrated SOC stack in a heat exchanger 
is described. The discretization of the heat exchanger and the assumptions are explained be-
fore describing the balances and equations for the discretized volumes. At the end of the chap-
ter, the implementation of the model into the used software “Engineering Equation Solver” 
(EES) is described. 
3.1 Discretization 
For the modelling of the integrated SOC stack, each layer (numbered with 𝑙= {1,2, … , 𝐿}) was 
regarded as a single heat exchanger. The different layers are connected to each other. For the 
hot fluid, the temperature and pressure at the outlet of the layer 𝑙  and at the inlet of layer 𝑙+ 1 
are set equal. Analogously for the cold fluid, the temperature and pressure at the inlet of layer 
𝑙 and the outlet of layer 𝑙+ 1 are set equal. Additionally, every layer is divided into 𝐾 ring-
shaped volumes (numbered with 𝑘= {1,2, … , 𝐾}) of the same height 𝐻𝑙. 
 
𝐻𝑙= 𝐻
𝐾 
(3.1) 
 
In this equation 𝐻 stands for the height of the stack and 𝐾 for number of discretizations in a 
layer. The numbering of the discretized volumes is following the flow direction of the hot fluid. 
The hot fluid is decided to enter the heat exchanger at the bottom of the first layer. The dis-
cretization and the numbering of the discretized volumes are depicted in Figure 3.1. 
 
Figure 3.1 Explanation of the discretization of the heat exchanger and the numbering of the discretized volumes

3.2   Mass balances 
8 
 
 
In Figure 3.2, nomenclature and numbering for the streams and properties, used in this work, 
are shown. In the figure, 𝑋 denotes any thermodynamic property used within this work, as for 
example mass flow 𝑚̇ , temperature 𝑇 or thermal conductivity 𝜆. The superscripts 𝐶 and 𝐻 
stand for a value of the cold and the hot fluid respectively. The superscript 𝑚 denotes a value 
in the “centre” of the discretized volume, i.e. not at the border of it. If there is no second super-
script, the quantity is valid at a border of the discretized volume. For the hot fluid, the subscript 
𝑙, 𝑘−1 denotes a value at the inlet of volume 𝑘 in layer 𝑙 and the subscript 𝑙, 𝑘 denotes the 
value at the outlet. For the cold fluid it is opposite, i.e. 𝑙, 𝑘−1 denotes a value at the outlet and 
𝑙, 𝑘 at the inlet. This numeration is used, because the values 𝑙, 𝑘−1 are calculated in the pre-
vious volume or for the first volume of the first layer they are given as boundary conditions.

For every volume, mass, energy and momentum balances are solved for the hot and the cold 
fluid. These are described in the following subchapters as well as the necessary auxiliary equa-
tions to solve the derived equation system. As a lot of the calculation are identical for the cold 
and the hot fluid in a volume, a new superscript 𝐹 is introduced, standing for 𝐶 and 𝐻 for the 
cold and the hot fluid respectively. If there is a difference between the calculations for the cold 
and the hot fluid, both equations are given with the corresponding superscript 𝐶 and 𝐻. For all 
calculations, the assumptions from Table 3.1 were applied. 
 
Table 3.1 Assumptions made within every discretized volume 
- 
The gases behave like ideal gases 
- 
Each fluid is ideally mixed within one discretized volume, i.e. homogenous fluid prop-
erties within the control volume. 
- 
Heat transfer through insulation layers is neglected 
- 
Steady-state conditions

3.5 Geometrical calculations 
The geometrical calculations are important, because the surface areas within the heat ex-
changer increases as more layers are used, due to the cylindrical geometry. This has an impact 
on the heat transfer and the mechanical strength. The first relevant diameter of the heat ex-
changer is the inner diameter of the steel shell on the inside of the first layer 𝐷0. As mentioned 
in Table 2.1 it is set to 𝐷0 = 0.8 m. 
Afterwards the numbering of the diameters is done from the inside to the outside. In order to 
introduce a general index for the diameters, only depending on the number of layer, in which 
the diameter is located, a sketch of a layer is drawn. This is depicted in Figure 3.4. It can be 
seen, that within one layer six different diameters appear. Therefore, the diameters can be 
written with the index (𝑙−1) ⋅6 + 𝑖, where 𝑖 denotes the number of a diameter within a layer. 
In Equations (3.15) - (3.20) the way to calculate each of the diameters in dependence of the 
previous diameter is shown.

In order to calculate the convective heat transfer coefficient, Nusselt-correlations are used to 
calculate 𝑁𝑢𝑙,𝑘
𝐹. There are different Nu-correlations for different flow conditions and boundary 
conditions. The flow condition is depending on 𝑅𝑒𝑙,𝑘
𝐹. Analogously to the flow in circular pipes, 
for 𝑅𝑒𝑙,𝑘
𝐹≤2300, the flow is laminar, for 𝑅𝑒𝑙,𝑘
𝐹≥104 it is turbulent and for 2300 < 𝑅𝑒𝑙,𝑘
𝐹< 104 
the flow is in the transition region between laminar and turbulent [9, Ch. Gb]. For each of the 
flow conditions, different correlations have to be used. 
The correlations used in this work are for constant wall temperatures. Thus, the number of 
discretization volumes K is chosen, so that the wall temperature for each volume is approxi-
mately constant. Additionally, the system is examined under steady-state condition, leading to 
a constant wall temperature over time. 
Three different boundary conditions, for which the heat transfer coefficient in an annular gap 
can be calculated, are depicted in Figure 3.6. In the first boundary condition, the heat transfer 
appears only on the inner surface of the annular gap, while the outer surface is adiabatic. The 
second boundary condition is the opposite of the first one. Here, the heat transfer is occurring 
only on the outer surface, while the inner surface is adiabatic. In the third boundary condition, 
the heat transfer occurs at the inner and outer surface and both surfaces have the same tem-
perature. This boundary condition is not relevant for this work and is therefore not explained 
further. 
 
Figure 3.6 Boundary conditions for the calculation of the convective heat transfer coefficient in an annular gap. 1. 
Heat transfer on the inner shell (index i), adiabatic outer shell; 2. Heat transfer on the outer shell (index a), adiabatic 
inner shell; 3. Heat transfer on both shells, same wall temperatures 𝜗𝑤𝑎= 𝜗𝑤𝑖 [9] 
As shown in Figure 3.5, the first and the second boundary condition can be found in every 
discretized volume. The convective heat transfer coefficient of the gas flowing in the inner gap 
is called 𝛼𝑖𝑛 and is calculated by Equation (3.38), with 𝑁𝑢in gotten from the Nu-correlations for 
the second boundary condition. The convective heat transfer coefficient of the gas flowing in 
the inner gap is called 𝛼𝑜𝑢𝑡 and is calculated by Equation (3.39), with 𝑁𝑢out gotten from the 
Nu-correlations for the first boundary condition.

or cold fluid in volume 𝑘 of layer 𝑙 have to be used.

stant temperature in every control volume was assumed. Therefore, it is decided not to use a 
correction of the calculated Nusselt-numbers and to use the mean Nusselt.

3.8 Calculation of the fluid properties of gas mixtures 
In the investigated system, all fluid streams are composed of different gases. Therefore, the 
fluid properties of gas mixtures are calculated. As mentioned in Table 3.1, the gases are as-
sumed to behave as ideal gases. In a mixture of ideal gases, the excess enthalpy ℎ and the 
excess volume 𝑣 equal zero leading to the simple equations shown in Table 3.2. The specific 
heat 𝑐𝑝 is calculated analogously to the calculation of the specific enthalpy, through Eq. (3.59). 
For the calculation of the dynamic viscosity 𝜂 and the thermal conductivity 𝜆 the mixing rules 
of Wilke and of Wassiljeva et al. are used respectively [10]. Both equations use the factor 𝐹𝑖,𝑗. 
It is calculated according to equation (3.62) and is depending on the dynamic viscosity and the 
molar mass of two different gases.  
In the equations of Table 3.2, the values with the superscript 𝑖 and 𝑗 denote the fluid properties 
of the pure gases in the mixture. The fluid properties of the pure gases are calculated in “EES” 
using correlations from McBride et al. [11] and Vargaftik et al. [12]. As the gases are assumed 
to behave like ideal gases, the fluid properties are only a function of the temperature 𝑇𝑙,𝑘
𝐹, ex-
cept of the specific volume, which is a function of the temperature 𝑇𝑙,𝑘
𝐹 and the pressure 𝑝𝑙,𝑘
𝐹. 
In order to keep the equations in Table 3.2 readable, the superscript for the fluid 𝐹 and the 
subscripts for the layer 𝑙 and the volume 𝑘 are not written. When using the equations, all val-
ues have to be used for the same fluid, layer and volume. 
𝜉𝑖 denotes the mass fraction of component 𝑖. The composition of the gases is gathered from 
the models of the electrical energy storage systems, simulated in DNA. The software works 
with molar fractions 𝑥𝑖 instead of mass fractions. Therefore, the data have to be converted 
using the molar mass of the component 𝑖 𝑀𝑖 and the mean molar mass of the mixture 𝑀. 
 
𝜉𝑖= 𝑥𝑖⋅𝑀𝑖/𝑀 
(3.56) 
𝑀= ∑𝑥𝑖⋅𝑀𝑖

3.10 Pressure loss 
Similar to the calculation of the convective heat transfer coefficient in an annular gap, special 
correlations for the pressure loss in an annular gap are used. As for the calculation of the 
convective heat transfer coefficient, the hydraulic diameter is an important value for the calcu-
lation of the pressure loss. It is calculated with equation (3.32). In Figure 3.7 and Figure 3.8 
the definition of the inner and the outer radius is depicted. However, analogically to equation 
(3.32) in this work the inner and outer diameter 𝐷𝑙
𝐹,in and 𝐷𝑙
𝐹,outare used. 
The important dimensionless number for the calculation of the pressure loss is the Reynolds 
number 𝑅𝑒𝑙,𝑘
𝐹. It is calculated with equation (3.35). 
All of the fluid properties have to be calculated in the “centre” of the volume. Accordingly, the 
values are calculated with the average temperature 𝑇̅𝑙,𝑘
𝐹, shown in Equation (3.36) [13].

3.11 Chemical reactions 
During the heat exchange inside the heat exchanger, chemical reactions can occur within the 
fluid. The reactions are not simulated in the described EES model. The reaction is first simu-
lated from the inlet temperature of the fluid, using the software DNA, in order to get the com-
position at different stages of the reaction, i.e. as a function of the temperature. This is done 
by using a component of DNA called Gibbs reactor. Therefore, the composition and tempera-
ture at the inlet of the reactor and the temperature at the outlet are given as inputs and the 
composition at the outlet is calculated by minimizing the Gibbs free energy. 
In Figure 3.10 the molar fraction of methane during a catalytic steam reforming starting at 
T=176.14 °C is depicted as an example. This data is then used in EES to interpolate the data 
in dependence of the temperature. This is done by using the interpolate function of EES, which 
uses a cubic interpolation. 
 
Figure 3.10 Molar fraction of Methane over temperature during catalytic steam reforming (assuming chemical equi-
librium) starting at T=176.14 °C and a starting composition of 49.55% CH4, 49.02% H20, 1.14% H2 and 0.29% 
CO2. (Simulated using DNA) 
==PAGE:135==
3   Mathematical model of the new heat exchanger design

3.12 Heat transfer through insulation 
As mentioned in chapter 3.3, the heat transfer through the insulation is neglected within the 
energy balances. In order to validate the assumption, the heat flow is calculated and compared 
to the heat transferred between the hot and the cold fluid. 
Compared to the heat transfer within the layers, there are two differences for the heat transfer 
through the insulation. Firstly, the heat transfer is always between the cold and the cold or the 
hot and the hot fluid, leading to smaller temperature differences. Figure 3.11 depicts the con-
figuration for the heat transfer through the insulation for both cases. Secondly, between the 
two fluids there are different materials, through which the heat is transferred by conduction, 
i.e. two steel shells and one insulation layer.

3.13 Mechanical strength of walls 
Due to the high pressure, at which the SOC stack is working, and the pressure differences 
within the SOC and the heat exchanger, problems with the mechanical strength of the walls 
can occur. The potential problems are yielding, buckling due to axial load from own weight and 
buckling due to external pressure. In order to avoid deformation or destruction of any of the 
walls, the critical thickness for the different potential problems is calculated for every discre-
tized volume. The calculations are only conducted for the heat exchange surface between the 
hot and the cold fluid and for the pressure-bearing steel on the outside of the integrated SOC 
stack.  
For the steel on the inside and the outside of every insulation layer, the calculations are not 
conducted. Reasons therefor are that the influence of the insulation and the pressure within 
the insulation are not known. A thickness of 𝛿Insulation
Steel
= 1 𝑚𝑚 is used. Further investigations 
on the influence of the insulation for the steel thickness should be done in following works.

crit,buckl,press.

3.13.1 Yielding 
When talking about yielding of metals one must distinguish between ductile and brittle metals. 
Stainless steel, which is investigated within this work, is a ductile metal. For this reason, only 
ductile metals are further explained in this chapter.  
Ductile metals are considered to have failed when a marked plastic deformation has begun, 
also called elastic failure. Under simple uniaxial tension this occurs at a relatively definite 
stress, called the yield strength or yield point 𝜎𝑦𝑖𝑒𝑙𝑑 [15, Ch. 3]. 
In this work, type 304 stainless steel is used as material. Data for the yield strength for different 
temperatures are taken from a product data sheet of the company AK Steel and depicted in 
Figure 1.1 [1]. In order to use the data for the computational solution of the problem, a polyno-
mial regression is conducted using the Curve Fitting Tool of Matlab. The used equation with 
an R-squared value of this regression is 𝑅2 = 0.9992 is

3.13.2 Buckling due to axial load from own weight 
Under certain circumstances the maximum sustainable load of an object is not determined by 
the material strength but by the stiffness. If there is a condition at lower stresses than the elastic 
limit, the condition is called elastic stability and the load that produces the condition is called 
critical load. Higher loads than the critical load usually leads to the collapse of the object or to 
local buckling, depending of the shape of the object [15, Ch. 3].  
In the case of a shell, buckling can occur when the axial stress 𝜎𝑙,𝑘
1 reaches the critical stress

4   Software and Implementation 
33 
4 Software and Implementation 
The model of the pressurized heat exchanger was implemented and evaluated in the software 
“Engineering Equation Solver” (EES). The basic function of EES is the numerical solution of 
linear and non-linear algebraic and differential equations. EES solves equation systems auto-
matically, i.e. the user does not have to develop iterative techniques in order to solve non-
linear equation systems. Another big advantage of using this software is the high quality prop-
erty database [16]. 
The software uses equations instead of assignments, as are used in standard programme 
languages. In assignments, each value on the right side of the equation must be determined 
previously, leading to the necessity of writing the term in an explicit way and solving the equa-
tion system sequentially. In contrast to this, equations are relationships between variables, 
which can be written implicitly and are solved simultaneously and iteratively. 
For the solution of equation systems, either the equations can be written directly into the equa-
tion window of EES, or Modules, Functions and Procedures can be used, in order to insure a 
readable code and a robust model. In this work, Modules, Functions and Procedures were 
used. In the following subchapters, the working principle of Modules, Functions and Proce-
dures and where they were applied in this work are explained. 
At the end of this chapter, the boundary conditions used for the solution of the equation system 
of the pressurized heat exchanger are described. 
The code of the implemented model in EES can be found in Appendix C. 
4.1 Functions and Procedures 
An EES function returns one single result and accepts one or more inputs. EES procedures 
are similar, but they can output one or more results [16]. The big advantage of functions and 
procedures is that they allow the use of logic statements, as If-Then-Else or Repeat-Until, 
which is not possible in the main body or in modules. In this work, If-Then-Else statements are 
necessary for the calculation of the pressure loss and the convective heat transfer coefficient, 
as there are different formulas used for different flow conditions. 
The pressure loss in the annular gap is calculated using a function with three If-Then state-
ments for calculating the friction factor as explained in chapter 3.10. For 𝑅𝑒< 3000 it is calcu-
lated with equation (3.70), for 3000 ≤𝑅𝑒≤105 with equation (3.71) and (3.74) and for 𝑅𝑒>
105 with equation (3.72) and (3.74). 
As explained in chapter 3.7, the calculation of the convective heat transfer coefficient is not 
only depending on the flow condition, but also on the flow configuration, i.e. if the heat transfer 
occurs on the inner or the outer surface of the annular gap. Thus, a procedure with one If-Then 
statement is used to determine which boundary condition is applied to the hot and the cold 
fluid and calls for the functions 𝑎𝑙𝑝ℎ𝑎_𝑖𝑛 and 𝑎𝑙𝑝ℎ𝑎_𝑜𝑢𝑡, in which the convective heat transfer 
coefficient is calculated, depending on the flow condition, which is determined with If-Then 
statements. For 𝑅𝑒< 2300 the equations from chapter 3.7.1 are used, for 𝑅𝑒> 104 the equa-
tions from chapter 3.7.2 and for 2300 ≤𝑅𝑒≤104 the equations from chapter 3.7.3. 
As for the convective heat transfer coefficient, the calculation of the overall heat transfer coef-
ficient depends on the flow configuration. In order to combine the convective heat transfer 
coefficient with the according heat transfer area in equation (3.29), a function with If-Then 
statement is used to calculate the thermal resistivity 𝑅𝑙,𝑘. 
Another function is used to calculate the logarithmic temperature difference with equation 
(3.10). This is necessary in the case of the same temperature difference on both sides of a 
==PAGE:144==
4.2   Modules 
34 
 
control volume because this leads to a division through zero. In this case, the temperature 
differs is not calculated with equation (3.10), but the actual temperature difference is used. 
An important property of functions and procedures is that they use assignments instead of 
equation. This is an advantage for some calculations, as e.g. for the calculation of fluid prop-
erties. Fluid properties are a function of the temperature, while the temperature is not directly 
dependent on the fluid properties. Thus, it is advantageous to avoid unnecessary iterations by 
using functions or procedures to calculate fluid properties. Therefore, functions are used for 
the calculation of the fluid properties of the gas mixtures (specific heat, specific enthalpy, spe-
cific volume, viscosity and thermal conductivity) and of the thermal conductivity of the insula-
tion.  
For the same reason, the composition in case of chemical reaction is calculated in a procedure. 
Additionally, an If-Then statement is used in order to fix the composition, if the temperature 
gets higher or lower than the upper or lower limit of the interpolation table. 
The geometrical calculations, described in chapter 3.5, are conducted using a procedure. 
Through the successive solution of the assignments it is insured, that the geometrical data are 
fix and only fluid data are iterated. This avoids divisions by zero, e.g. in equation (3.30).

4.2 Modules 
EES modules are similar to procedures. They can return one or more results and accept one 
or more inputs. In contrast to procedures, modules use equations rather than assignments. 
The equations can be entered in any order in implicit form [16]. One advantage of using mod-
ules is that not all equations are written in the main body of the EES code, but are written in 
the modules ahead of it, so that the main body is easier readable. Another advantage is that 
==PAGE:145==
4   Software and Implementation 
35 
modules can have their own start values and upper and lower limits. This is especially advan-
tageous if the same equations are repeated several times within one model, as it is the case 
in this work, where each energy and momentum balance is conducted for each control volume. 
This can be used to make the model more robust to imprecise guess values and divisions by 
zero. Some of the most important upper and lower limit settings for a robust model are shown 
in Appendix C, Figure C.2 and Figure C.3. 
In this work, two modules are used, one for all equations concerning the energy balance and 
one for the momentum balance. In these, all calls for the functions and procedures of the pre-
vious chapter and all other equation from chapter 3 are included. 
In Figure 4.1, a flowsheet of the implementation of the equation system in EES is shown. The 
grey ellipses denote the modules used in the model, the grey rectangles the procedures, the 
white rectangles the functions and the white rectangles with dashed lines simple equations 
used within the modules. The white rhombuses denote the input values for solution of the 
equation system of one control volume and the dark rhombuses the outputs. 
In the next subchapter the boundary conditions for the whole system and the in- and outputs 
of each control volume are described. 
4.3 Boundary conditions and inputs 
For the solution of the equation system, boundary conditions are necessary. In Figure 4.2, left, 
a basic sketch of the counter flow heat exchanger is depicted. Usually, for the calculation of a 
counter-flow heat exchanger, the inlet temperatures 𝑇𝐶,inlet and 𝑇𝐻,inlet are given as boundary 
condition and 𝑇𝐶,outlet and 𝑇𝐻,outlet are calculated for a given geometry. The equation system 
is then easily solved for one heat exchanger. In the moment, in which the heat exchanger is 
divided into more than one volumes, it gets more difficult, because none of the control volumes 
has the same number of equations and variables. This leads to the necessity of solving one 
big equation system for all control volumes and that can lead to numerical difficulties during 
the solution. 
 
Figure 4.2 left: Counter-flow configuration used in the model of the heat exchanger; right: nomenclature of streams 
entering and exiting volume k in layer l 
This can be avoided by fixing the temperatures at the hot end 𝑇𝐶,outlet and 𝑇𝐻,𝑖𝑛𝑙𝑒𝑡. This assures 
that each control volume has the same number of unknowns and equations. Thus, the control 
volumes can be calculated sequentially with smaller equation systems for each control volume. 
In Figure 4.2, right, one control volume is depicted. For each volume, the temperatures at the 
left end, 𝑇𝑙,𝑘−1
𝐻
 and 𝑇𝑙,𝑘−1
𝐶
 are known inputs and the temperatures at the right side are outputs, 
which are used as inputs for the next control volume. The same approach is used for the 
calculations of the pressures. The pressures at the hot end 𝑝𝐶,outlet and 𝑝𝐻,inlet are used as 
boundary conditions. 
As mentioned previously, this approach is only feasible for a known geometry. Thus, the ge-
ometry is an input to the model. The necessary inputs are the inner diameter 𝐷0 = 0.8 𝑚, the 
==PAGE:146==
4.3   Boundary conditions and inputs 
36 
 
gap widths 𝛿𝑙
𝐹, the insulation thickness 𝛿Insulation and the thickness of the steel walls 𝛿stand
Steel and 
𝛿𝑙
Steel.  
As described in chapter 3.13, the thickness of the steel walls between the fluids 𝛿𝑙
𝑆𝑡𝑒𝑒𝑙 has to 
be calculated, in order to insure the mechanical stability. These calculations are not conducted 
in the EES model, but iteratively in an external Excel-file, i.e. the wall thickness is first guessed, 
then the calculations are conducted in EES and the mechanical stability of the steel walls is 
checked with the Excel-file. If necessary, the thickness is increased and the calculations are 
repeated. 
==PAGE:147==
5   Mathematical model of a reference case using a commercial pressure vessel and an 
external plate heat exchanger 
37 
5 Mathematical model of a reference case using a commer-
cial pressure vessel and an external plate heat exchanger 
In order to compare the results of the integrated SOC stack in a heat exchanger, a reference 
case is evaluated. The reference case consists of a pressure vessel similar to the one currently 
used at the Department of Energy Conversion and Storage of the DTU, shown in Appendix B, 
and a plate heat exchanger in which the heat exchange between the hot and the cold fluid is 
conducted. 
5.1 Pressure vessel 
For the reference case, two different pressure vessels were examined. One using water as 
cooling agent and one using the cold gas before it enters the heat exchanger. The advantage 
of the first one is that they are the commercial standard of pressure vessel and can be bought 
directly from manufacturers. The second one brings advantages for the system, as it uses the 
heat for the process and it avoids the additional use of cooling water. 
5.1.1 Pressure vessel cooled with water 
Commercially available pressure vessels with the necessity of cooling of the pressure bearing 
shell are usually cooled with water. An example of a commercial pressure vessel, cooled with 
water is shown in Appendix B. For the application of high temperature pressure vessels, an 
additional insulation can be added between the hot centre of the stack and the pressure bear-
ing steel in order to assure low temperatures. In Figure 5.1, left, the design idea for this work 
is shown, using an additional insulation layer. The pressure bearing shell is the layer between 
the insulation and the water channel. It has to support an internal pressure difference of Δ𝑝 
between the stack pressure 𝑝𝑖𝑛 and the water pressure 𝑝𝑤𝑎𝑡𝑒𝑟. The steel layer on the inside of 
the insulation is necessary for preventing the gas from diffusing through the insulation. In order 
to avoid a load on the outer shell between the cooling water and the environment, a water 
pressure of 𝑝water = 1 bar is used. 
 
 
 
Figure 5.1 Top view of the configuration of a pressure vessel cooled water, the dark grey layer depicts the pres-
sure bearing shell; right, sketch of the heat exchange between the cold water end the hot gas surrounding the 
SOC stack 
==PAGE:148==
5.1   Pressure vessel 
38

3 are used. The heat transfer between the hot gas surrounding the stack and the cooling water 
can be modelled according to Figure 5.1, right, and calculated with

Any chemical reactions that could occur in the cold gas are neglected. Technically this is easily 
feasible, by not using catalytic material in the gas channel. 
5.1.2 Pressure vessel cooled by cold gas 
Similar to the integrated SOC stack in a heat exchanger, the pressure vessel could be cooled 
on the outside by using only one channel through which the cold fluid flows before entering the 
heat exchanger. 
In Figure 5.2, left, the design idea of the pressure vessel cooled by the cold gas is depicted. 
As for the water-cooled vessel, an insulation layer is introduced between the hot stack in the 
centre and the cold gas flowing in the channel inside of the pressure bearing steel, in order to 
reduce the heat loss of the SOC stack. The pressure bearing steel is the layer on the outside 
of the gas channel. It has to support an internal pressure difference of Δ𝑝 between the cold 
gas 𝑝𝑖𝑛 and the environment 𝑝𝑒𝑛𝑣. 
 
Figure 5.2, left Top view of the configuration of a pressure vessel cooled by the cold gas before it enters the heat 
exchanger, the dark grey layer depicts the pressure bearing shell; right, sketch of the heat exchange between the 
cold gas end the hot gas surrounding the SOC stack 
==PAGE:150==
5.2   Plate heat exchanger 
40 
 
The calculation of the pressure vessel cooled by cold gas are done identically to the calcula-
tions of the pressure vessel cooled with water, shown in chapter 5.1.1. The model is built ac-
cording to Figure 5.2, right. 
In contrast to the pressure vessel cooled by water, the steel shell on the outside of the insula-
tion does not bear any load. Thus, a thickness of 𝛿out
Steel = 1 mm is sufficient to prevent the gas 
from diffusing through the insulation. 
Another difference to the calculations is that in this case the outlet temperature of the gas is 
not set to a constant value. Instead, the mass flow is fixed according to the data from the model 
of the energy storage system. 
As in the calculations of the water-cooled pressure vessel, the insulation thickness 𝛿𝐼𝑛𝑠𝑢𝑙𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛 
is a design variable, used to achieve a defined heat loss 𝑄̇ Loss of 𝑥Loss percent of the power 
input of the system in electrolysis operating mode 𝑊̇ SOEC. 
The thickness of the pressure bearing steel on the outside of the pressure is calculated using 
the equations from chapter 3.13. For the calculations, the highest temperature of the wall 
𝑇𝐶,outlet is used. 
5.2 Plate heat exchanger 
When using a commercial pressure vessel, the heat transfer between the hot fluid exiting the 
SOC cell and the cold fluid entering does not happen within the shell. Hence, an additional 
plate heat exchanger is required. Previous investigations at the Department of Energy Con-
version and Storage of the DTU showed, that an external heat exchanger working at ambient 
conditions on the outside and with 𝑝≈20 𝑏𝑎𝑟 and 𝑇≈700 °𝐶 at the inside is not possible. 
Therefore, the heat exchanger must be placed inside the pressure vessel. This introduces two 
problems for the reference case system. Firstly, the pressure vessel must be higher than for 
the integrated design in order to fit the heat exchanger. Secondly, commercial heat exchangers 
are usually not produced to work at surrounding temperatures of 700 °C. For such elevated 
temperatures usually specially designed and expensive heat exchangers are used. However, 
within this work the calculations of the plate HEX are conducted assuming commercial heat 
exchangers in order to get a feeling for the size and the pressure loss. 
For the calculation of the plate heat exchanger used for the heat transfer between the hot fluid 
exiting the SOC cell and the cold fluid entering, the SSP G7 software of the company SWEP 
© is used. The software is developed for calculating single-phase, condenser, evaporator, cas-
cade or two-stage applications and proposing suitable products of SWEP © [17]. 
In Figure 5.3, a screenshot of the graphical user interface of the software is depicted. On the 
left side of the window, the input parameters are entered. The necessary input parameters 
vary, depending on what the software should calculate.  
In order to find a suitable heat exchanger for a defined heat transfer between two fluids the 
“Design” mode is used. Therefore, the fluids have to be chosen and the total heat transfer has 
to be determined by entering five values between the inlet and outlet temperatures of the fluids, 
the mass flows of the fluids and the heat load. Additional the maximal pressure drop within 
each fluid can be entered. The software determines seven suitable heat exchangers and gives 
the pressure drops of each fluid, the oversurfacing (OS) and the weight of the heat exchanger. 
Additionally, a price factor rating is given. This is a relative value, comparing the proposed heat 
exchangers to each other. On the result page, links to the product sheet of the heat exchangers 
are available, where information about the pressure bearing ability and the measures of the 
heat exchangers can be found. 
==PAGE:151==
5   Mathematical model of a reference case using a commercial pressure vessel and an 
external plate heat exchanger 
41 
 
Figure 5.3 Screenshot of graphical user interface of the software SSP G7 of the company SWEP © [17] 
In the “Performance” and “Rating” mode a selected heat exchanger is evaluated for given fluids 
with the respective temperatures and mass flows. This can be used to get the pressure drop 
and the oversurfacing of a heat exchanger that is not perfectly suited for the application. 
The software is built to calculate and propose heat exchangers for pure substances. However, 
in this work mixtures of gases are used. In order to use the software for this case, the built-in 
Fluid Editor is used. In the Fluid Editor, new fluids can be added to the library by entering fluid 
properties manually. For single-phase applications, the density 𝜌, heat capacity 𝑐𝑝, thermal 
conductivity 𝜆 and kinematic viscosity 𝜈 are entered for different temperatures. The fluid prop-
erties of the mixture of gases are calculated using the equations from Table 3.2. 
If in one of the fluids a chemical reaction takes place, the specific heat has to be determined 
differently. When a reaction takes place, the total enthalpy between two states Δ𝐻 consists of 
the enthalpy difference due to temperature differences Δ𝐻therm and the enthalpy of reaction 
Δ𝐻react. 
 
𝑚⋅Δℎ= 𝑚⋅𝑐𝑝∗⋅Δ𝑇= Δ𝐻= Δ𝐻therm + Δ𝐻react = 𝑚⋅(Δℎtherm + Δℎreact) 
(5.14) 
 
This is used to calculate an apparent specific heat capacity 𝑐𝑝∗ 
 
𝑐𝑝∗= Δℎ
Δ𝑇= Δℎtherm + Δℎreact
Δ𝑇
= 𝑐𝑝+ Δℎreact
Δ𝑇
 
(5.15) 
 
The reaction is simulated with the software DNA, as explained in chapter 3.11 and the enthalpy 
is extracted as a function of the temperature. In Figure 5.4, the specific enthalpy of a gas 
mixture during a catalytic steam reforming starting at T=176.14 °C is depicted as an example.

6   Results for electrical energy storage system without biomass gasifier 
43 
6 Results for electrical energy storage system without bio-
mass gasifier 
The design of an integrated SOC stack in a heat exchanger is examined for the electrical 
energy storage system without a biomass gasifier, designed by Butera [2],[3]. The flow sheets 
for the fuel cell and electrolysis are shown in Appendix A, Figure A.1 and Figure A.2. The heat 
exchanger fulfils different tasks for the different operation modes. In the electrolysis mode there 
occurs only heat transfer between the hot fluid coming from the SOEC and the cold fluid going 
to the adiabatic methanator before entering the SOEC. In the design case of Butera, the total 
heat transferred within the heat exchanger is 𝑄̇SOEC = 5.98 MW and the temperature difference 
at the pinch point is Δ𝑇Pinch,SOEC = 53,5 °C. 
In the fuel cell operating mode, the HEX is also used for a catalytic steam reforming in the cold 
fluid, in order to increase the concentration of CO and H2 in the gas before entering the SOFC. 
As the steam reforming reaction is endothermic, the heat transfer in this case is higher than in 
the SOEC mode with 𝑄̇SOFC = 11.68 MW. Additionally, the temperature difference at the pinch 
point is lower with Δ𝑇Pinch,SOFC = 30 °C. These values lead to the expectation, that the heat 
transfer area for the fuel cell operating mode is much bigger than for the electrolysis mode. 
For this reason, the calculations are first conducted for the fuel cell mode. Then the design is 
applied to the electrolysis mode and if necessary changed in order to assure to be applicable 
for both modes. 
6.1 Fuel cell operating mode 
In the fuel cell operating mode, a catalytic steam reforming occurs within the heat exchanger. 
Therefore, it is expected, that due to the high required heat transfer the heat transfer area is 
big. In order to evaluate the influence of the big heat transfer area on the size of the heat 
exchanger was calculated using the model explained in chapter 3. Thereby, each layer was 
divided into 𝐾= 10 volumes in order to have a detailed resolution of the catalytic reaction. First 
the influence of the thickness of the annular gap was examined, by varying it for a HEX of a 
height 𝐻= 1 m. Then the 1 m HEX was calculated as a base case with the most suitable gap 
width. Afterwards a variation of the HEX height was conducted and the influence of the different 
insulation thicknesses was investigated. 
The mass flow of the hot and the cold fluid for different HEX heights were calculated from the 
mass flows 𝑚̇ DNA and active areas 𝐴DNA
SOC  used by Butera, using Equation (3.6a) [3].

The influence of the thickness of the annular gap 𝛿𝑙

𝐻= 𝛿𝑙
𝐶= 𝛿𝑙 and varied between the minimal allowed gap width of 𝛿𝑙= 1 mm and

In Figure 6.3, the heat transferred within the first heat exchange layer is depicted for different 
gap widths. It can be seen that it decreases with increasing gap width, from 𝑄̇(𝛿𝑙= 1 mm) =

==PAGE:156==
6.1   Fuel cell operating mode 
46 
 
 
Figure 6.3 Heat transfer within the first heat exchange layer of a SOFC stack with H=1 m for different gap widths 
The decreasing heat transfer at bigger thickness of the annular gap leads to a smaller decrease 
of the temperature of both fluids, as shown in Figure 6.4. On the ordinate of this figure, the flow 
pass length is given. For a HEX height of 1 m, one meter of flow pass length coincides with 
one passed layer. 
As the temperature difference between the two fluids at the hot end of the heat exchanger is 
the same for all gap widths, the product of overall heat transfer coefficient and heat transfer 
area 𝑈𝐴 must be bigger for smaller gap widths, according to equation (3.9) 
 
𝑄̇𝑙,𝑘= 𝑈𝐴𝑙,𝑘⋅Δ𝑇l,k
log  
(3.9) 
 
 
Figure 6.4 Temperature profile of the hot and the cold fluid in the first heat exchange layer of a SOFC stack of 
H=1 m for different gap widths 
Figure 6.5 shows that 𝑈𝐴 in fact decreases with increasing gap widths. As shown in Equation 
(3.29), 𝑈𝐴 depends on the convective heat transfer coefficient, the heat transfer area, the 
thickness and the thermal conductivity of the steel wall. The thickness of the wall was set to a 
constant value of 𝛿1
Steel = 1 mm for all cases and has therefore no influence. The thermal con-
ductivity depends only on the temperature of the both gases, which at the hot end of the heat 
exchanger are equal and therefore are not a reason for the differences. 
 
==PAGE:157==
6   Results for electrical energy storage system without biomass gasifier 
47 
 
Figure 6.5 Development of the overall heat transfer coefficient 𝑈𝐴 in the first heat exchange layer of a SOFC 
stack of H=1 m for different gap widths 
The development of the mean heat transfer area, calculated with Equation (3.30), is depicted 
in Figure 6.6. It can be seen that the area increases with increasing gap width. This is because 
a bigger gap width leads to a bigger inner diameter of the steel shell separating the two fluids. 
An increasing heat transfer area leads to an increase of 𝑈𝐴. 
 
Figure 6.6 Mean heat transfer area of the first heat exchange layer of a SOFC stack with H=1 m for different gap 
widths 
Nevertheless, as shown in Figure 6.7, the convective heat transfer coefficient in the hot and 
the cold fluid decrease strongly with increasing gap widths. In order to understand the reason 
for this, the different parameters, influencing the convective heat transfer coefficient have to 
be examined.

change layer of a SOFC stack of H=1 m for different gap widths 
By rearranging equation (3.33), it can be seen that the convective heat transfer coefficient 
increases with increasing Nusselt numbers and thermal conductivities, while it decreases with 
increasing hydraulic diameters.

while the hot fluid is in the transition region between laminar and turbulent flow.

discretized volume, the Reynolds number stays approximately constant for different gap widths 
and thus, the friction factor is approximately constant too. Due to the similar temperature at the

pressure differences in the first control volume of the first heat exchange layer are given for

creasing gap width. The decrease derives from the lower fluid velocities due to a bigger cross-
sectional area. The potential pressure difference is approximately constant. The differences of 
the potential pressure difference come from the slightly different specific volume due to differ-
ent temperatures at the cold and of the control volume.

The negative signs of the kinetic pressure difference in the hot fluid and the potential pressure 
difference in the cold fluid denote that the kinetic or potential pressure decreases with the flow 
direction. In the hot fluid, the negative sign derives from the decrease of the temperature in 
flow direction, which leads to a decrease of the specific volume and the fluid velocity. The 
negative sign of the potential pressure difference in the cold fluids comes the fact, that in the 
first layer, the cold fluid flows from the top of the heat exchanger to the bottom and therefore 
the “hydrostatic” pressure of the fluid decreases.
